        "You can find my review on my blog by clicking here.Everyone is a goddamn phony. It‚Äôs almost impossible to finish this book without having that sentence engraved in your thoughts, forever. The Catcher in the Rye was one of my most surprising reading experiences yet. It‚Äôs not your conventional young adult story. It is a book that has known censorship before embracing immense fame. It‚Äôs popularity grew at an exponential speed and ended up getting incredible praise among teenagers. How? It was all iYou can find my review on my blog by clicking here.Everyone is a goddamn phony. It‚Äôs almost impossible to finish this book without having that sentence engraved in your thoughts, forever. The Catcher in the Rye was one of my most surprising reading experiences yet. It‚Äôs not your conventional young adult story. It is a book that has known censorship before embracing immense fame. It‚Äôs popularity grew at an exponential speed and ended up getting incredible praise among teenagers. How? It was all in Holden Caufield, the protagonist of The Catcher in the Rye, and how so many adolescents were able to relate with him. This story is a glimpse into the life of Holden Caufield. He narrates the events, one after the other, and puts forth his entire thought process in black and white. After learning that he once again got kicked out of high school, Holden Caufield decides to follow his gut feeling and takes a trip to New York City. Through these events, readers uncover a child who‚Äôs simply falling into adulthood. In this story marinating in a theme of coming of age, Holden Caufield is an elegy for adolescents. It‚Äôs through his confusion, his angst, his pain, his pleasures and his desires that we see his struggle to remain a child, yet swimming in a world of adults.As I was reading, I thought I was going to end up hating this book. Everything about it seemed to be paving the way for the hate to roar out of me. There‚Äôs no exposition, there‚Äôs no climax, there‚Äôs no denouement and there‚Äôs no ending. The whole book is a sequence of actions told through the voice of Holden Caufield and his particular writing style. His hypocritical behavior, his cynicism, his repetitive language, his sarcasm, his swearing and his ability to digress are all there to strain you and build your patience for this character. You wouldn‚Äôt believe me if I told you the number of times he might have gotten on my nerves. Just his abusive use of ‚Äúthat killed me‚Äù would give me a brain aneurysm. Although it was somehow compelling to read about Caufield‚Äôs thoughts on a person or on a thing in particular, you always felt like he wasn‚Äôt leading you anywhere. His ability to digress is absolutely stunning. He could see a person at a bar and end up bringing you on a journey through memory lane. It‚Äôs only after finishing The Catcher in the Rye that it hit me. I actually did enjoy this. I actually did appreciate the thoughts of Holden Caufield and the way J.D. Salinger delivered this cynical child‚Äôs story. I actually did understand what Holden Caufield was going through. I felt like I had told parts of my own childhood to him and fell in this trap of getting attached to him. In the end, Holden Caufield was right. ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt ever tell anybody anything. If you do, you start missing everybody.‚ÄùHolden Caufield is without a doubt the biggest phony out there. But why he is such a person is where things become so much  interesting. There isn‚Äôt a moment throughout the book that you won‚Äôt see him pointing fingers to every phony out there. Even in today‚Äôs society, there‚Äôs no denying the number of people who put on a farce and pretend to be someone they‚Äôre not or say things that they don‚Äôt honestly believe in. Even if Holden Caufield is able to see through these people, making it even  obvious that his adulthood is catching up to him, he does the exact same thing throughout the book. In fact, he blatantly admits to being one of the biggest liars out there. But it‚Äôs this irony and this contradiction that makes his adolescent self so intriguing. The way he perceives life brings him to alienate himself from the world and not bother with people who can‚Äôt stay true to themselves. In fact, I found that Holden is a strong believer of talking from the heart, of saying things that bring your excitement and leaves you in a peaceful trance. But what he believes and what he does are two completely different things, since he too has difficulties expressing himself or even a disinterest to do so. Holden Caufield is a very complex character who‚Äôs easily seen by many readers as a rebel who doesn‚Äôt want to do things that he doesn‚Äôt feel like doing. His desire to live in the present and to do things that simply pop into his mind makes his adventures so spontaneous.It‚Äôs heartwarming to see Holden Caufield want to preserve the childhood of every kid out there. Without explicitly saying so, you can tell from his behaviors and beliefs that it is something very important for him, especially when he‚Äôs starting to realize that he had already fallen off the cliff. His wish to be that person to stop others from falling off the cliff into adulthood is something that can easily strike a chord among readers. Even if Holden Caufield remains the biggest phony in this story, he somehow managed to make me like the guy. The Catcher in the Rye has been a controversial novel that has managed to join the list of banned books in history. It‚Äôs depiction of a cynical teenager who tackles themes of sexuality, drinking, profanity and many other things has made this a book that could stir the morals and values of a lot of individuals. Just to make it even  shocking, I‚Äôve read that this book has even made the headlines alongside killers. The biggest story related to The Catcher in the Rye is the death of John Lennon. Mark David Chapman, the infamous killer who took Lennon‚Äôs life, was arrested with a copy of this book bought on the same day he killed him. Inside the book he left this note: ‚ÄúTo Holden Caufield, From Holden Caufield. This is my statement.‚Äù Now, is being able to relate to the character a good or bad thing? I‚Äôll let you find out.Yours truly,Lashaan | Blogger and Book ReviewerOfficial blog:  is a goddamn phony. It's almost impossible to finish this book without having that sentence engraved in your thoughts, forever. This book was one of the most surprising reading experiences yet. As I was reading, I thought I was going to end up hating it. Everything seemed to be paving the way for the hate. There's no climax, there's no denouement, there's no ending whatsoever. There is a cynical teenager who narrates the events of a couple days after being kicked out for the billionth time from high school. His hypocritical behavior, his cynicism, his repetitive language, his sarcasm, his swearing and his ability to digress are all there to strain you and build your patience for this character. The whole book is a collection of events seen through the eyes of Holden Caufield. It's only after finishing The Catcher in the Rye that it hit me. I actually did enjoy this. I actually did appreciate the thoughts of the character and the way J.D. Salinger delivered his story. I actually did understand what Holden Caufield was going through.Holden Caufield is without a doubt the biggest phony out there. But why he is such a person is where things become so much  interesting.P.S. Full review to come soonYours truly,Lashaan | Blogger and Book ReviewerOfficial blog: ",
        "In junior high I had a crush on a guy about two years older than I was who rode the same bus. He had curly dark hair, and wore black sweaters and always carried a notebook that he scribbled in. Poems? Personal essays? I never dared to ask. \"So Byronic!\" I would have sighed, had it been a couple of years (and about fifty Marion Chesney romances) later. So this guy, let's call him Byron since I have many Goodreads friends from Rexburg, was reading this book on the bus one day. \"Oh,\" I said, daringIn junior high I had a crush on a guy about two years older than I was who rode the same bus. He had curly dark hair, and wore black sweaters and always carried a notebook that he scribbled in. Poems? Personal essays? I never dared to ask. \"So Byronic!\" I would have sighed, had it been a couple of years (and about fifty Marion Chesney romances) later. So this guy, let's call him Byron since I have many Goodreads friends from Rexburg, was reading this book on the bus one day. \"Oh,\" I said, daring to speak to him, wanting him to know that I, too, was a Reader, \"I haven't read that yet. What is it about?\"Byron looked up at me and sighed. \"It's not for a CLASS. I'm just reading it.\"\"Ah, yes?\" My little heart went pitter pat. I, too, read books that weren't for class! This was Meant to Be.\"It's about a kid who wanders through New York, wondering where the ducks on the pond go in the winter,\" Byron finally said.Pause.\"He wonders where the ducks on the pond go in the winter?\" What? I tried for witty. \"My guess is south!\" Ha ha ha ha!Sigh. \"It's not ABOUT the ducks.\" Turns back to the book.\"What is it about then?\" No answer.Byron spent the rest of the school year reading CATCHER IN THE RYE on the bus. He looked so deep and artistic in his black sweaters, reading that deep and artistic book. Years later I would realize that he was faking it. The book is less than 300 pages long. He was either the world's slowest reader, or he was just carrying it around to look edgy. By then I was so over him anyway. That was pretty much the only time I ever talked to him, and two months later he told a mutual friend that I bugged him constantly on the bus. Exsqueeze me? Baking powder? And then I went to high school, and I discovered something about THE CATCHER IN THE RYE. It was a prop. It was what kids carried around to look rebellious and intellectual. The book wasn't banned at our school, it was actually on every reading list for just about every English class. They just never taught it in class, because in Rexburg no teacher would dare to require students to read a book that had swears, smoking, and drinking it. There were even rumors of . . . SEX! I've just shocked you all, I know. No regrets.Several of my completely normal friends read it for their outside reading in Jr Honors or AP English. They didn't start smoking cigarettes and calling everyone phonies. But then there were the kids who did. The kids who carried it outside their backpacks all the time, for months and months, but wouldn't talk about it if you mentioned it. The implication was that you weren't smart enough, or cool enough, to understand it on the level that they did. They were reading it as part of their plan to Stick it to the Man, not because they needed to write another essay for Mrs. Johnson.So I pretty much wanted no part of that. I challenged my friend Cade (real name!) to a contest to see who could read the most books off the AP list. He won. (I think the score was 37-43.) I don't think either of us read THE CATCHER IN THE RYE. I went to college. It was never required, because I was in the Humanities. And I was fine. And then a few years ago it started to bother me that when I saw beautiful, exciting lists of classic books, or great American novels, I had read so few of them! And I decided to rectify that!So here we are.And I, for one, am glad that I read this book now, and not back then. I am also 99.9% sure that Byron and the rest of his ilk did not understand it. This is not a book about sticking it to the Man. This is a book about loss, and being lost. It's interesting to see now how many books there are like this. John Green has become a bestseller writing about disaffected, chain-smoking teenage intellectuals. But in 1950? No, this was not something that you saw. I can understand why it struck a nerve, and why it's stayed current. It was fairly undated, with the exception of a couple of slang terms. I honestly thought I would hate it, and I didn't. I quite, quite liked it. I mean, it's never going to be in my top ten, but I was surprised by how much I liked it. And how much I liked Holden, and how much I worried about him. He was truly a lost soul. Broken by his grief for his brother Allie, feeling abandoned by his older brother, dumped at a series of boarding schools by his parents, he needed someone to connect with, to talk to, to care about him. And the book is all about him. Where he goes and what he does doesn't matter, the point of the book is that you are watching him come undone. That and making you look cool carrying it around I guess.",

        "I disliked this text while I was reading, but I grew to appreciate aspects of it while talking about it with other people once I had completed it. My first thought after finishing this novel was that I did not understand how is has such an iconic status. I think I might have liked the book better if I had not picked it up having heard for years how life changing it was.\"Catcher in the Rye\" is redundant, irritating, and so introspective at times as to be an unlikable reading experience, but I thiI disliked this text while I was reading, but I grew to appreciate aspects of it while talking about it with other people once I had completed it. My first thought after finishing this novel was that I did not understand how is has such an iconic status. I think I might have liked the book better if I had not picked it up having heard for years how life changing it was.\"Catcher in the Rye\" is redundant, irritating, and so introspective at times as to be an unlikable reading experience, but I think that was Salinger's point. The novel's protagonist (Holden Caulfield) is a typical teenage navel gazer who derides everyone else for being part of the problem, while never taking the time to look in the mirror. He is unlikable and selfish although in his heart he does not want to be either of those things. And I guess that is why in the end I like this novel. Holden is a person who wears many masks, but he does not want to. He yearns for real human connection, but he does not know how to create it. That is a problem that most people can relate to. Throw into the mix Holden's questionable sexuality, the death of a younger brother, his painful feelings of isolation and loneliness, and you have one very sad character, and one very human story.This book is not a novel that has an especially interesting plot, or any sort of convenient conclusion. However it is an interesting study in character, and in that most basic of human desires, the ability to connect with others, and the pain that often follows when you allow yourself to love and be loved.I am almost positive I would not have appreciated this book as a teenager, and I am glad that I waited many years before reading it. I would recommend reading it with others so that you can discuss it, and get past its unlikable characters. In a group dynamic  of the novel's humanity will come out and your appreciation of it will be better.On a side note, the way that Mr. Salinger incorporates the novel's title is very touching, and I think a very important theme of the text. Read it and see what you come up with.",

Yeah, me neither.This is the book my history teacher described as the quintessential book of the 1950‚Äôs. I remarked that we were reading it in English currently. Many of the kids laughed and eagerly nodded along.I was probably the only one who was never once tempted to slap Holden, or to tell him to just grow up, or yell at him to get his shit together. To be honest, I kinda wanted to give the guy a hug.Holden Caulfield is a 16 year old boy, and like many of us at 16, he is struggling immensely with the fact that he is growing up. Coping (well, actually not coping at all) with the loss of his brother and continually being kicked out of schools for poor grades, Holden takes off for a few days in New York City rather than heading home and having to face the disappointment of his parents yet again.This book surprised me so much. I could see how much thought was put into the story. I mean, it‚Äôs so symbolic. When one of our essay options was to write an entire essay just on Holden‚Äôs hat, you know this book has depth. I went back and found some insane foreshadowing as well. It has so many opportunities to create brilliant discussions between readers.I think the story has a unique character voice and Holden is incredibly developed and complex. He‚Äôs not a perfect person; he‚Äôs judgemental, impulsive, and can be horrible to those around him.But, to be honest, I saw myself a bit in Holden. Okay, a lot. Occasionally I did stop and think to myself please tell me I don‚Äôt sound like this. Holden struggles so much to perceive the world around him, realizing as he grows up just how dark it can be. Maybe it‚Äôs because Holden and I are at similar ages, finding our places in the world.I just loved the overall tone of this story, which could be funny but also had this somber undertone that made the story seem beautiful beyond its crude language and angry narrator. It was those sad little passages that reflected the complexity of life that made me fall in love with this story.I also am kind of astonished that there was a mental health themed story out at this time. Holden, no doubt, is suffering from depression. I love mental health novels, so that was just an added bonus.This book is a character study at its best. Plot? Nah. This book is just about Holden running around New York, not really doing anything, but that‚Äôs completely fine by me. Some of the best stories just explore life without much happening.That reminds me; this book reminds me so much of one of my all time favorite novels, The Spectacular Now. Honestly, so similar; both coming of age character studies about a spiraling teenage boy. Love both of them, so if you love one I imagine you‚Äôll at least enjoy the other.Also, is this technically the first YA book? I‚Äôve heard that title go to The Outsiders many times, but this book is 30 years older and most definitely YA if you ask me.Anyway, I‚Äôm really glad I read this book. The story beA couple of days ago, a friend handed me a copy of ‚ÄòThe Catcher In The Rye‚Äô and said,‚Äô Read it with care.‚Äô I didn‚Äôt know what to make of it. Probably he was alluding to my slight aversion to YA. But I decided to heed his advice, but turns out it was not needed. TCITR (as I will be referring to this book from here onwards) is not like any YA found on the bookshelves of today, which though not necessarily ‚Äòbad‚Äô aren‚Äôt really to my taste. But this one certainly sets the bar quite high. The story begins with Holden Caulfield, our brash and narcissistic narrator, recounting the events of three days of his life starting with the day he decides to ‚Äòleave‚Äô school. Well, he decides to leave earlier than intended considering the school kicked him out. I should also mention that this was not the first school that he was kicked out of. But that wouldn‚Äôt faze our fearless teen angst ridden quixotic Caulfield. He perceives the institutions and its occupants ‚Äòphony‚Äô disguising their selfish endeavors and with gentility. I won‚Äôt reveal  of the story but suffice it to say that the story pretty much follows Holden after his escape from the ‚Äògraveyard of phonies‚Äô to New York. There‚Äôs drinking, bars, cigarettes, sex and wild abandon. And right there in the hotchpotch, Salinger addresses so many issues and not in the manner of waving the flag right in your face but with so much delicacy, it permeates all the  deeply for it. What  could you want! What struck me throughout the story is the excess use of the word ‚Äòphony‚Äô that is used to describe everything that the child in Holden despises. In fact I believe the child within was the narrator that was in constant loggerheads with the inevitability of the future that ushered adulthood. Adulthood represented artificiality, a joyless, dark and gloomy world. And even so, a constant gloom seemed to be in the very backbone of the story. Which makes sense now that I know the story was supposed to been written from a mental hospital. Another thing( in a long long list) that struck me was how brash and cocky H sounded even though through his own voice it was so clear that he suffered from insecurity issues and felt alienated from a world that didn‚Äôt quite ‚Äòget‚Äô him. His constant criticisms served as a reflection of his own inadequacies. I found the book like looking into the mind of an adolescent boy without any filter, hence it very beautifully brings forth the deficiencies of mental faculties, not yet refined thoughts and the delicate lack of empathy so true at that age. The tone in the book was monopolized and while to some it may seem quite repetitive, was for me the best tool in the story. It was unadulterated and an excellent coming of age thing. And oh yeah, the book was depressing. How lovely! P.S.: If I wasn‚Äôt clear enough already- J.D.Salinger is a genius. Thank you!",

        "So I just woke up. Still in my pajamas. And with only half of my brain working. So I thought to myself: I'm just in real prime condition to review a classic. Yeah.Whatever. Let's do this.The story is about the controversial Holden Caufield, and all the angst and teenage alienation that he felt in the world. I've read the book way, way back and never really gotten into writing a review until now. I submitted papers to school, of course, but I've stolen those from the internet.Now. Holden CaufieldSo I just woke up. Still in my pajamas. And with only half of my brain working. So I thought to myself: I'm just in real prime condition to review a classic. Yeah.Whatever. Let's do this.The story is about the controversial Holden Caufield, and all the angst and teenage alienation that he felt in the world. I've read the book way, way back and never really gotten into writing a review until now. I submitted papers to school, of course, but I've stolen those from the internet.Now. Holden Caufield. I've read a lot about the fictional guy. In between and out of the Catcher in the Rye pages. People either see him as a hero (mostly teenagers) or see him as a whining bitch (mostly grow-ups), which in my opinion is what makes the book a classic. When, after decades, your story can still get a rise out of people and has the ability to start a heated parent-child argument... you're immortalized in the literary world.Well,I'm nothing if not argumentative. But truth is, I've never gotten into any argument about this book. Yet. The thing is I don't hate Caufield. And I also don't idolize him. What I feel toward the sarcastic, undecided, lonely guy is pity. While reading the book, I feel so sorry for him I almost cried. He is just a kid and he just needs attention. He still grieves about his brother's death. He doesn't know what to do with himself nor with his life. He might or might not like this girl but he can't even make himself ring her up. It's not like I don't ever see myself in him. I do too. I was a teen once and I'm also that undecided. I also depress about everything. I also thought my problems and issues must be and should be the greatest, most important thing happening in the universe. But I'm 21 now so i'm all wise and you know, mature-not. Just a little different really. Because we all change, which is by the way brings me to sharing these lines that just about killed me. \"You'd just be different, that's all. You'd have an overcoat this time. Or the kid that was your partner in line the last time had got scarlet fever and you'd have a new partner. Or you'd have a substitute taking the class, instead of Miss Aigletinger. Or you'd heard your mother and father having a terrific fight in the bathroom. Or you'd just passed by one of those puddles in the street with gasoline rainbows in them.\"When I first read the book I also waited for something big to happen in the story, some crazy twist, some clear, well-defined plot line. Salinger would have none of it. All throughout it's just Caufield telling us about himself, confusing contradictions, self-inflected problems, baseball glove, and red hunting hat. At first, this is a let-down for me. But then I just had an epiphany. (See what I did there? Throwing big words when I'm not sure what else to say.) I realized that this is the genius of this book. It's about nothing particular, just life, which could be something or everything. Or yeah, nothing at all.When I think about it, really think about it, the message of this book for me is: It's okay. Be confused. Be misunderstood. Be sad. Make mistakes. Screw up your life. You'll change. Maybe learn. What's black and white is that you'll change. You'll get over yourself. What I wish is, what we very uncertain and young and stupid people wish is, we could play and run in the open field, and never fear of falling through, and knowing that somebody would be there for us- to be our Catcher in the Rye.Now forgive this review if it doesn't make Any sense at all. Like I've said, I just woke. Now I'm going back to sleep.Zzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzzz....",

So when I finally got around to reading it as a 36 year old mother of 4, I was thrilled to be finding out what all the fuss JESUS WEPT.Now I know why a movie has never been made of The Catcher in the Rye - because even Zac Efron wouldn't want to be in it. The reason I've given this book one star is because Goodreads have yet to develop the Negative Ratings system, but I'm sure they'll catch up soon.This is a 'seminal classic', or so every hipster worth his bicycle basket would have you believe. So when I finally got around to reading it as a 36 year old mother of 4, I was thrilled to be finding out what all the fuss is about. All I can say is, thank God I've read it for the first time at an age where I can articulate my emotions, because if I had read it in high school, I'm pretty sure the entire world would have be reduced to pink mist at the frustrated and disgruntled hands of my AK-47.It slices me in half that this drivel is so lauded when there are kindergarten kids writing perfectly good sentences about whales and bananas which never make it out of the classroom, much less onto a 100-books-to-read-before-you-die list. There were a couple of astute observations, and there are several passages that are quite amusing, but the problem is that by the time you get to them, you are so atrophied with boredom that your body no longer remembers how to laugh and can only emit a slow, pained whine instead. Or maybe that was just Holden, moaning again?There is absolutely nothing this character does not complain about. I'm pretty sure that if a clown tried to give him a balloon filled with 40 million dollars, Holden would call him a phoney and tell him to fuck off. Are we supposed to be surprised that he's actually a virgin? Of course he is. Imagine him having sex with someone - \"My orgasm was pretty crummy. How was yours goddammit. Wait...you didn't have one? ARE YOU A PHONEY? Boy, that kills me, if you want to know the truth. I mean. Boy.\"No wonder JD Salinger became a recluse after this became so successful. I'd be embarrassed too.",

        "(Excerpt from a long essay review. See the link at the end.)Sometimes, I wonder if we‚Äôve already fallen off the cliff. Holden Caulfield was supposed to catch us long ago; we were never supposed to choose life, washing machines, junk food, and mindless game shows--and yet we did anyway. We were supposed to die glorious deaths and live on forever way before we ever fell off the cliff. Yet here we are: we‚Äôre falling, and our falling is so quiet and mundane that by the time we‚Äôre about to hit bottom(Excerpt from a long essay review. See the link at the end.)Sometimes, I wonder if we‚Äôve already fallen off the cliff. Holden Caulfield was supposed to catch us long ago; we were never supposed to choose life, washing machines, junk food, and mindless game shows--and yet we did anyway. We were supposed to die glorious deaths and live on forever way before we ever fell off the cliff. Yet here we are: we‚Äôre falling, and our falling is so quiet and mundane that by the time we‚Äôre about to hit bottom we‚Äôve forgotten that there was a time we used to run around free in rye fields. You have no idea what I‚Äôm talking about, do you? I suppose I‚Äôm talking about Salinger‚Äôs Catcher in the Rye, and what the main character, Holden Caulfield, wanted to do when he grew up. He wanted to work in a rye field and catch kids right before they ran off the cliff into an untimely oblivion. It‚Äôs that moment in the novel that gives the book its title. I keep picturing all these little kids playing some game in this big field of hay and all. Thousands of little kids, and nobody‚Äôs around--nobody big, I mean--except me. And I‚Äôm standing on the edge of some crazy cliff. Besides, it's public knowledge that John Lennon's killer, after shooting him, was waiting for the police with this novel in his hands. He does so using the language available to him, one of a teenager's own, with all that that implies (insults, repetitions, references to the sexual acts and transparency). In his ideas and conceptions about the people he interacts with and the places he transits to, is hiding a strong social critique that is worth discussing as a reader.I believe that the author managed to write with solidity from the voice of a teenager and capture the extremism, spontaneity and emotional lability that many of us have probably experienced as we went through that stage of our lives.I found many mixed reviews of this book but personally I enjoyed reading it so much and kept wondering why I hadn‚Äôt picked it up earlier.",

        "Seinfeld ruled 90s television's ratings and many argue that it was the greatest sitcom of all-time, embracing that it was \"a show about nothing.\" The Catcher in the Rye is a random novel written in the early 50s by a J.D. Salinger, whose career was not very prolific and his novel has since become a book on 99.99% of high school curriculums that just about everyone reads. This is what I see as being \"a novel about nothing.\" However, the idea that Seinfeld follows the life and times of Jerry SeinfSeinfeld ruled 90s television's ratings and many argue that it was the greatest sitcom of all-time, embracing that it was \"a show about nothing.\" The Catcher in the Rye is a random novel written in the early 50s by a J.D. Salinger, whose career was not very prolific and his novel has since become a book on 99.99% of high school curriculums that just about everyone reads. This is what I see as being \"a novel about nothing.\" However, the idea that Seinfeld follows the life and times of Jerry Seinfeld and his gang and The Catcher in the Rye follows Holden Caulfield brings about the postmodern argument that there really is nothing special about any given life and that in the life of the common man, others will simply see it as nothing. In the case of a teenager, who in many cases life is all about them, the postmodern argument displays otherwise. This novel was written right at the peak of postmodernism, too.Let's talk about Holden Caulfield. I saw him as being a clear misfit and a bad influence. He smoked, drank underage, lied to get his way, got into fights, failed in all except one of his classes, and was just plain rude to just about everyone he came across. He is telling the story and he does not really defend his actions, which I feel cannot be defended. The one thing that really stuck out to me, though, was his love for his siblings. He had a clear sense of affection for his deceased younger brother, Allie, and his living and feisty younger sister, Phoebe. His older brother, D.B., was a Hollywood screenwriter, whom he liked, but tended to find a bit over the top. His interactions and moments where he was with Phoebe are clearly the best parts of the novel. Holden engages in plenty of adventures that are scattered across the novel without a driving plot. He meets teachers at their homes, engages in banters with his roommates, dances with women about ten years older, meets with nuns, and comes into contact with a prostitute... and it really amounts to nothing.While The Catcher in the Rye can definitely be relatable to teens and strikes chords where it is designated to do so, I cannot say that this was an enjoyable novel and that Holden Caulfield was an enjoyable character. Then again, I am 24 and would never engage in most of the things Holden did. The two of us have nothing in common, but still, I could not see where Holden would be likable. He has traits that people can relate to, but they are clearly not for me. The most I could recommend The Catcher in the Rye for is a light rent or a cheap buy. I do not find this to be a full price buy nor do I feel that teachers should be held to the standard of assigning this novel. I would much rather prefer that Robert Cormier's novels, specifically I Am The Cheese if we want something with a similar tone to this novel, be assigned instead. Cormier's novels are much  honest AND straightforward than The Catcher in the Rye and I feel that this is what should be displayed in what is assigned in high school. This looks much  like a random selection.It can also be assured that Mark David Chapman is just a renowned cuckoo. Why this novel would inspire him to kill John Lennon is beyond my wildest beliefs...You can find the discussion I had about this book on Literary Gladiators here: ",

        "This book gets a lot of flak because its protagonist, Holden Caulfield, is crude and random in his thought patterns. But that's only a superficial view. A careful reading of the book shows that underneath all of the swearing, sour opinions, and teenage angst, is the story of a boy who has been traumatized. He has PTSD, particularly as a result of the death of his brother Allie and another boy at one of his former schools, James Castle. As his mentor tells him, Holden is \"confused and frightened This book gets a lot of flak because its protagonist, Holden Caulfield, is crude and random in his thought patterns. But that's only a superficial view. A careful reading of the book shows that underneath all of the swearing, sour opinions, and teenage angst, is the story of a boy who has been traumatized. He has PTSD, particularly as a result of the death of his brother Allie and another boy at one of his former schools, James Castle. As his mentor tells him, Holden is \"confused and frightened and even sickened by human behavior\" (246), and this is the reason for his \"bad\" attitude and disruptive behavior. The phoniness of the world makes him sick, literally so, and reviewing his troubled memories is part of his personal healing. In fact, far from being \"immoral,\" Holden is probably the only MORAL person of the book (aside from his little sister Phoebe). He is the only one who recognizes how false and pretentious people can be, and is also the only one who calls the world out for it. I get that the content of this book may not be appropriate for younger audiences, and others may balk at the frequent cussing. But let's not make the mistake of thinking Holden is rotten ‚Äî he's the only one in this book who has a soul.",

        "After being sucked into Holden's stream of consciousness for the last two days and just now coming up for air, I suppose there are two or three things I'd like to mention.The goddamn ducks, obviously. The cab driver had it all wrong, and all Holden wanted was a straightforward answer. The mallards that fleck the North American landscape are sensitive to changes in the food supply and model their migratory patterns accordingly, not unlike Homo sapiens prior to the advent of agriculture. For most After being sucked into Holden's stream of consciousness for the last two days and just now coming up for air, I suppose there are two or three things I'd like to mention.The goddamn ducks, obviously. The cab driver had it all wrong, and all Holden wanted was a straightforward answer. The mallards that fleck the North American landscape are sensitive to changes in the food supply and model their migratory patterns accordingly, not unlike Homo sapiens prior to the advent of agriculture. For most of their history, humans have hunted and gathered where there was food to hunt and gather, schlepping off to new haunts as the seasons and weather dictated. It's not that ducks don't have the ability to withstand the wintry conditions. Indeed, their thick feather coats serve as nature's perfect insulators. Rather, they follow the food. During the northeast winter, insects, fish and aquatic plants like duckweed become scarce as cold temperatures transform the local ecology.Annual changes in duck concentrations can also provide clues as to the cold conditions from which they flew. A harsher winter will bring  birds south to the Mississippi River Valley, for example, to feed on plants and grass not covered by snow and in open waters not glazed over with ice. Due to the weakening of the polar jet stream brought about by changes in the global climate, we've been seeing  ducks nestled along the southern locales of North America in recent years.That was all probably  than you wanted to know, but if someone had explained this to Holden in grade school it would have been one less thing bouncing around his busily eccentric mind.Troubled YouthWhich brings us to Holden Caulfield himself and the man who created him. What strikes me about this novel is the nagging question of whether we are glimpsing inside the mind of Salinger or whether Holden is a cold-cloth fabrication. I suppose anything a person writes is a glimpse into their mind in a way, but we are always left to wonder how closely a book's characters resemble their creator. Is Holden's brooding disaffectedness a reflection of a lifelong angst and alienation suffered by Salinger?Salinger was of course the quintessential enigmatic recluse, but that hasn't stopped commentators from speculating. We know he was born and raised in New York City, that he performed poorly in school‚Äîso much so that his father sent him to military academy in Pennsylvania as a last resort‚Äîand that he later joined the Army during World War II. Like so many others, the war seems to have had a profound and lasting influence on Salinger, who was hospitalized for nearly a year in Nuremberg. The unauthorized biographer Ian Hamilton suggests Salinger suffered a nervous breakdown in July 1945, which may explain Salinger's letter to friend Ernest Hemingway in which he mentioned almost being discharged from the Army on psychiatric grounds.Autobiographical or not, Salinger's Caulfield is among those rarefied characters who have managed to compel readers for generations. Even those who don't necessarily connect with Holden tend to find his angst-ridden mirror on society irresistible. I don't see much of myself in his chronic repining over things big and small, and still I was drawn to him, like a car crash from which you can't quite look away. The \"plot\", if we can call it that, hardly extends beyond the inner recesses of Holden's mind. Indeed, his vortex of dread and relentlessly wry running commentary is the plot. He's the directionless teenager we all love to hate, and I think it's because wrapped up in Holden's embittered, existentialist essence is a version of our past or present selves we can't help but identify with.I sincerely wanted Holden to course-correct and not let his overpowering discontent get the better of him. Maybe he would have been better off with a healthy dose of SSRIs and some counseling, or perhaps‚Äîgiven the subversionary role reversal in which he and his sister Phoebe are engaged in the closing section‚Äîhe just needed to see himself from the outside looking in. The Streisand EffectI dug around a bit trying to find out why I didn't read this book in high school like everyone else. As it turns out, The Catcher in the Rye is one of the most censored books in U.S. history, which would certainly explain its absence on the reading list at the ultraconservative Christian school I attended. Apparently Holden's rebellious nature was (and still is) thought to be too infectious, his vocabulary too potty-mouthed and the sexual references too saucy. Atheism also pops up sparingly, with Holden self-identifying as an atheist and attributing the label to his brothers as well. Alas, it's safe to assume this numbered among the reasons many a school board fought for its proscription.Of course, the Streisand effect all but guarantees that banning a work of art will only lift its status far higher than it may otherwise have achieved. In any case, Salinger's best-known work is no worse for the wear, and excels in its own right. The frosty Holden is as captivating as he is cynical, and his penchant for slang and low-brow humor make for easy reading. It might have taken me longer than most to read this literary classic, but it was well worth the wait.Note: This review is republished from my official website.",

Yes, Holden's lingo is annoying Well, I never thought I would be rating this book 4 Stars, but I sure am. To me, Holden Caulfield came across as an extremely sad, depressed and lonely sixteen year old who is distraught over the death of his younger brother Allie resulting with an inability to interact with people or cope with everyday life. Even with his obvious psychological problems, he exuded intelligence and was compassionate towards the Nuns and small children as well as his sister Phoebe. Yes, Holden's lingo is annoying and abusive, but its fast pace throughout certainly fit his emotional roller coaster.Originally published in 1951, I found the book insightful and quite relevant even today.         "For me, the beauty of this is its deceptive simplicity. Catcher is so readable and so accessible that people can approach it from a hundred different angles. Catcher is so readable and so accessible that people can approach it from a hundred different angles. For some it's allegorical, for others it's comedic, still others find it sad or disturbing. And then there are those like Mark David Chapman, John Hinckley, Jr., and Robert John Bardo who have unfairly made it a footnote for perversion and murder. Are we projecting something of ourselves on Salinger's canvas? I think maybe we are. I found this similar to another favorite novel of mine, Phillip Roth's 'Indignation' - both feature a teenage misfit who, in failing to assimilate at his expensive, predominantly white prep school, embarks on a path of self destruction. I can relate. My own life has been a procession of cyclical misadventures. I am sympathetic to Holden because he is dysfunctionally functioning - mostly by just being kind. He'll loan you his turtleneck even if he barely knows you, and he'll drop $10 in the nun's basket without being asked, and he'll watch over old Phoebe because he loves his little sister. Yeah, his future isn't all that bright, but his heart's in the right place and I'm a sucker for underdogs with good intentions.\"It rained all over the place. All the visitors that were visiting the cemetery started running like hell over to their cars. That's what nearly drove me crazy. All the visitors could get in their cars and turn on their radios and all and then go someplace nice for dinner - everybody except Allie. I couldn't stand it. I know it's only his body and all that's in the cemetery, and his soul's in heaven and all that crap, but I couldn't stand it anyway. I just wish he wasn't there. You didn't know him. If you'd known him, you'd know what I mean.\" (pg. 202)",

I can't say I agree with that at all. Sure, he's 'rebellious' - a word that I often hear being thrown around when people talk about teenage years - but for me being a teenager definitely hasn't felt like it did for Holden. He's essentially everything I am not, personality-wise.I did find it slightly aggravating how Holden kept making comments on things relating to homosexuality. I understand that this book was published in 1951 and things were slightly different then, but I still don't think it's acceptable. Holden keeps picking fights, doesn't keep many opinions to himself, and is extremely hypocritical and negative, but I still found myself sympathising with him a few times. Especially because he constantly seeks human interaction but keeps being met with the raw distance that is stuck between him and the rest of the world, leaving him lonely and secluded after each conversation. Holden's thoughts are laid bare on each page; nothing is held back. He tells everything he feels and sees - everything that is true to him. Even things he himself doesn't completely understand.Salinger really did something with this one: Teachers were fired (and reinstated, luckily), copies of his book were removed from schools in the United States (The Catcher in the Rye ended up being one of the most censored books of the twentieth century), and John Lennon's assassin was found reading the book out loud to himself after the murder.That last piece of information fried at least a few of my brain cells.",

Also he has been expelled out of a school for fourth time in a row.The story revolves pretty much around Holden, his encounters with different people like his history teacher, his dormitory mates, mother of a guy who studies with him, a prostitute, his younger sister etc. After an uneventful night at dormitory he decides he‚Äôs had enough of this place and will go to Manhattan three days early, stay in a hotel, and not tell his parents that he is back. When he arrives at destination, he goes into a phone booth and considers calling several people, but for various reasons he decides against it. Finally he hires a cab and checks in at some lousy hotel. He spends time drinking at a bar, having unwanted events unfolding around him and finally go to sleep. Next day he calls up a girl he used to date, Sally, and convince her to go on a date with him, but the date goes wrong and he ends up offending her big time.Then he meets his old student adviser, who is now a student at University. The story is simple. During the course of a weekend, school senior Holden Caulfield gives us an account of his life up to that point as he leaves the school from which he has been expelled. He tells of his roommates and teachers and his older brother RB, a big writer in Hollywood; Phoebe, his adored younger sister and Allie, his younger brother who died. Holden has a number of desultory encounters through the course of the several days; with roommates, a teacher who probably wants to molest him, some out of town girls in a bar and most poignantly with Phoebe as he sneaks into his family home, anxious to avoid his parents for as long as possible once they learn of his latest expulsion.I am about 40 years too old to get the maximum benefit from The Catcher in the Rye. To get the most out of it you probably have to have been a teenager in the fifties. Reading the book when you are old and full of life‚Äôs experiences, irrespective of how you have handled them, allows some detachment. We know how youthful anger, uncertainty, na√Øve hopefulness (witness the time when Holden wants to go away ‚Äì anywhere, he just wants to go off somewhere with Sally - the idea quickly comes to nothing) and a  or less permanent state of directionlessness (I hope that is a word), and lack of fulfilment, can change over time. We know these feelings modify and dissipate over the years as hopes fade or a calling is found, skills are acquired and concrete obligations arise and have to be met. In short, we cope.Holden doesn‚Äôt cope. He does not apply himself. He is all over the place. He has been profoundly affected by the death of his younger brother. I was worried he was going to take his own life.I have to imagine how impactuous this book was in 1951. I know impactuous is not a word but Adele, one of my collaborators, used it when we were reviewing a film and when I said ‚ÄòI don‚Äôt think that‚Äôs a word, she replied, ‚ÄòWell, it should be!‚Äô The Catcher in the Rye was impactuous then and still is now.",

Salinger. The story takes place in mid 1950‚Äôs in New York City, where adolescent Holden Caulfield roams around searching for a place to fit in. When Holden learns that once again he has been kicked out of the prestigious private all boys school in which he is enrolled in for this month, he decides to leave the quiet school life and return home once again to New York City.Throughout this novel, I found many interesting aspects. The dialogue that Salinger wrote for Holden is truly capturing, and as a reader I begin to feel as though I grew up next door to the Caulfield‚Äôs my entire life. There is something about the way the main character goes about things, in such ways that no other person would be likely to think of. Holden is extremely dramatic, making it comedic and enrapturing for the reader. It was also interesting the amount of low self-esteem for which J.D Salinger provided for his main character. Holden is often depressed and this is clearly expressed at many points in the novel. Holden uses sarcasm, and although once again comedic, it makes it difficult for a reader to decide whether or not he is thinking. This point leads us into the next idea about J.D Salinger‚Äôs The Catcher in the Rye, his weaknesses. As hilarious and awkward Holden Caulfield was throughout the novel, as a reader, a majority of my time in the novel I felt confused and unable to keep up with the main character. Continuously, I would find myself re-reading pages, and even chapters to attempt to understand what was going on in Holden‚Äôs life at that moment. Another weakness clearly expressed was the fact that there was hardly a plot. Yes, it may be na√Øve that I like a love story, and a happy ending, but this novel had neither. It did not have so much as a rise or fall of the protagonist. In fact, it was as though there was a rollercoaster of rises and falls for the protagonist, but none of them significant. This caused me to be bored with the novel, and once again have trouble getting through it without drifting off to other places in my mind.Although it may be true that a large portion of the United States thought that this novel was excellent and recommend it to all coming of age teens, I for one did not enjoy this novel; despite the comedy and intrigue.         "I first read this book in September, 2015. It's been almost two years since I last read this book.Like so many of other readers, I initially HATED this book. There was seriously NOTHING I could identify myself with, and my Goodreads review back then goes like this:Quite honestly, I'm not even sure what to make of this - in the beginning, until I reached almost the half of the book, I was kind of mad at Holden because- I swear - there was NOTHING I could relate to him; I neither got to understoodI first read this book in September, 2015. It's been almost two years since I last read this book.Like so many of other readers, I initially HATED this book. There was seriously NOTHING I could identify myself with, and my Goodreads review back then goes like this:Quite honestly, I'm not even sure what to make of this - in the beginning, until I reached almost the half of the book, I was kind of mad at Holden because- I swear - there was NOTHING I could relate to him; I neither got to understood his feelings nor felt like trying to see things from his angle.Yet, when I was past 70 percent of the book, the closer it got to the end, the less disturbed and disgusted I became.Although I wouldn't go so far as to say I like this book, I wouldn't mind it as much as I did in the beginning.I may even go back to this book sometime later in my life, who knows I won't?I had thought that I would hate this book as much as I did the last time, but quite surprisingly, I actually enjoyed this reread.First and foremost, his narration didn't bother me as much; I clearly remember feeling frustrated and irritated by his insolent attitude as though he was making fun of everything, but with this reread, I was curious - the entire time - to know why he acts the way he does, I wanted to pin down where his sarcastic, sort of jaded perspectives come from.The narrator of this book and the protagonist, Holden Caulfield, basically lashes out at everything, literally EVERYTHING. Once he finds something that he doesn't like, he knocks it right off the bat and nothing escapes his cynical, sarcastic eyes.At the same time, however, he is sensitive and vulnerable; even the smallest of things can trigger his mood swings and his emotions and feelings fluctuate by the second.¬†One moment he despises everything he sees, but the next minute something tugs his heartstrings and he becomes quite compassionate and appreciative.I think this second-by-second emotional fluctuation comes from his observant nature and is well reflected in somewhat desultory narration. We get to see how his mood swings and how depressed he gets through his soliloquy, but the author stops short of revealing Holden's true emotions and the reasons of his actions until we are well into the book. All I got to have up until that point was Holden's inexplicable, pent-up frustrations and angst the source of which I can't put my fingers on. Some of you may find this rather frustrating, but that actually worked well for me and made me power through this book.Like I said earlier, the writing reflects his vacillating emotions and his seemingly stuck-up nature really well. He has a very distinct voice and his perspectives may come across off-putting at first, but as I went deeper into the book, his compassionate, soft nature gradually came into view. I even find him far from apathetic or indifferent. Rather, I assume he is incredibly honest and doesn't want to do anything at a surface level; if he does do something, he wants to put his mind to it. That is where his aversion to phoniness comes from and it was masterfully narrated. Believe it or not, I was impressed by the writing. I was so invested in Holden's character this time.Although I still don't know where his angst and undirected emotions come from, I thought what he fears the most was 'change' or 'growth.'Whenever he observes others acting differently than before, he feels depressed and lonely. While he wants them to stay the way they used to be, they rapidly go through their transitional phase from childhood and adulthood.He doesn't want to grow up but he needs to grow up. Such ambivalent, conflicting emotions and the fear of failing to catch up with others torments him and drives him to his impulsive itinerant actions.¬†I might be wrong, but that's how I viewed him.Again, there are so many things that I still can't put my finger on as to Holden's emotional process, but his desperate attempts to sate his emotional starvation was acutely felt this time. \"I mean if they're running and they don't look where they're going I have to come out from somewhere and catch them. That's all I'd do all day. I'd just be the catcher in the rye and all. I know it's crazy, but that's the only thing I'd really like to be. I know it's crazy.\"Sadly, however, it was up to this point I really enjoyed reading this book.¬†This sequence, where Holden decides to go back to his house and sneak into Phoebe's room was a watershed moment for me both in a good way and a bad way.¬†The dialogue that Holden has with Phoebe was pretty eye-opening and enlightening. Phoebe's character setting is just wonderful. She is smart as a tack for a 10-year-old, I personally found Phoebe much  mature than Holden. A simple sentence, just one single sentence she mentions here cuts right through the truth and takes Holden by surprise; triggered by her question, Holden starts to gush about the reason why he detests phoniness with his own words and this is where I felt that I partially understood Holden.I thought I would end up liking this book much better than the last time, but then again, there comes another twist and I was yanked back to the square one - another litany of rambling which I felt kind of sorry given the incident he went through but I felt my interest rapidly withering away. The ensuing 20 pages until the end felt pretty tedious and drag if you want to know the truth.¬†The ending is kind of hopeful, it does end on a positive note. However, I still felt the ending a bit anticlimactic, or rather, it still leaves a lot of unanswered questions for me, it's almost akin to indigestion and I was kind of sad about not being able to say 'I loved it!' with this reread. Since I was enjoying this book until pretty close to the end, I really wish I could say that I come to love this book.That said, I am glad that I got to better appreciate this book than the last time.¬†Apart from the ending, I really enjoyed reading this and I thought it is well-written and gripping.It definitely has a strong first half and only for that, I think I can raise my rating exponentially from 2 stars to 3.5 stars.¬†If you go into this book knowing what kind of character Holden is in advance, you might not be put off by it as much.¬†I put my much delightful reread down to having built up a stronger threshold for Holden.¬†",

He doesn't want to apply himself to his classes and thinks everyone at Pencey Academy is a phony. After a fight with one of his roommates, he packs his bags and leaves.The school had kicked him out anyway because he flunked all of his classes besides English. He's got two days to kill till his parents expect him at home. What can a teenage, hormonal boy do but get drunk, attempt to flirt with older women, and, as Holden frequently puts it, horse around? May I be the millionth one to ask how in the world this ever became a classic? I suppose this is the gruesome birth of YA novels. It really surprised me how similar it was to how YA novels are written today. Imagine a John Green book, then put it together with The Perks of Being a Wallflower and this is the love child. I swear, it was like Stephan Chbosky read the book ten times and then wrote The Perks of Being a Wallflower. (although the writing style was actually much better in The Catcher in the Rye, if I'm honest) It was as if the author had gotten drunk, sat down, and wrote all night, and in the morning, The Catcher in the Rye was the result. Holden, besides being a compulsive liar, among other things, was a bit (understatement) mentally unstable. Everything depressed him and he thought about death all the time. He thought about how things would be if he died, how his family would react, the people who would show up at his funeral, etc. Then he'd turn around and say he didn't want to die or 'disappear'.Sometimes Holden would say something completely and utterly true as to how people think or what people do and act. Some of the things he thought were uncannily true/accurate observations of human nature. Then he'd say or think something that would be completely and utterly stupid and you'd think, what the hell, man? To Holden, everyone was either a phony, a moron, a whore, or just plain stupid. He was extremely judgmental. He judged people immediately on how they either looked or acted. But what people look like or even how they act is not necessarily a key to how they truly are. Maybe that's what the book was trying to say; how when you meet someone, you immediately make judgements about them. You can't help it, your mind does it almost automatically. That's what Holden represents, perhaps. I don't know. Maybe Holden was just kind of an asshole.Holden, to me, was an asshole, and at the same time, I kind of liked him. I pitied him. More or less. He was a depressed boy, and one who didn't know what to do with his life. Leaving school and being on his own for a few nights made him realize how depressing the world was when you looked at it. He felt like how a lot of teenagers do: that school is pointless and you feel invisible and just want to fit in. Everything's multiplied: Your hormones, your fatigue, your emotions, your judgements, etc. Small actions by others can set you off, either positively or negatively. The only thing I knew about this book before I went into it was that the main character's name was Holden. That's it. Now I know that it's about a boy named Holden who goes around smoking cigarettes, getting depressed, and being judgmental. Some parts of the book I really liked, and other parts irritated me, or were depressing. I have very mixed feelings about it. I really loved the second to last chapter, with Phoebe and Holden. What I didn't get was why Holden started getting angry with her all of the sudden when he got out of the museum. Why he felt such sudden hatred towards his little sister and even wanted to hit her. What the hell was that about?But my favorite scene came soon after that, when Phoebe put the hunting hat on Holden's head. It was so sweet. It's not a very pleasant book to read, I'll say that much. Holden is mentally and physically sick and his judgmental comments would be insulting to a lot of people. But then again, he's actually pretty funny sometimes. There were a couple nice sayings that were quoteworthy but it's still not much different a typical John Green novel nowadays. I'm guessing it was probably the inspiration for Green to write his books like he does. It's how the mainstream authors got started. It's one of those books that I liked and disliked at the same time. Very conflicting emotions. (view spoiler)[ I borrowed my copy from the library, and the book was written all over in the margins. But you know how Holden says 'goddamn' all the time? Well, someone had tried to cross out all the 'god' parts in 'goddamn'. Someone else erased the marks but you could still clearly tell what they had done. Lmao here. (hide spoiler)]",
        "I have read this book ten times now and I am so emotional about it that I don't even know what to say, but it's amazing and I am never going to love anything ever in the world as much as this book because it is beautiful okay peace out.***Catcher is my number one, favourite book of all time. This time reading it I annotated and made notes through-out, and so instead of trying to write the review that I have spent years trying to articulate (and which will be majorly biased anyway), I thought I'dI have read this book ten times now and I am so emotional about it that I don't even know what to say, but it's amazing and I am never going to love anything ever in the world as much as this book because it is beautiful okay peace out.***Catcher is my number one, favourite book of all time. This time reading it I annotated and made notes through-out, and so instead of trying to write the review that I have spent years trying to articulate (and which will be majorly biased anyway), I thought I'd just share the things I picked up on from my ninth reading of this novel. - Reference to David Copperfield at the start both links it to and separates it from classic bildungsroman tales- Holden plays a classic childlike game of catch outside an academic building, only to be told by a teacher to stop. A symbol of the swelling pressure of academia and adulthood on his life.- The Egyptians are so important for a number of different reasons. Firstly, Holden is fascinated by them because of their ability to preserve bones, translating to his need to preserve the youth and innocence of others. Holden then 'teaches' this to two young boys in a museum near the end of the novel, symbolically becoming the role of the teacher himself and thus closing up the circle of innocence-to-experience that he comes to terms with by the end. - Holden is an extremely unreliable narrator,  unreliable than I first ever noticed. Sometimes it's to 'hoarse' around but other times it's to save his reputation, like when he says he only threw up after drinking because he 'made' himself throw up. As well as this being an obvious lie, it disconnects him from those he sees abusing alcohol and convinces us (badly) that he is not one of them.- To have sex is to 'give someone the time', perhaps because it's a sign of growing older and maturity. Represents a moving forward of time.- Dancing is linked to Phoebe which is then linked to innocence. He likes girls who can dance because he likes the feeling of happiness he gets from dancing with his little sister. It is a sigh of youth and freedom.- Only one person in the whole book asks him if he's on his own, and he completely avoids her question and doesn't answer. - The way he talks about women is objectifying, infantilising and vaguely rapey.- References to the war link the trauma of war to the corruption of innocence, such as when Holden won't let Allie play with his BB guns because they are a symbol of violence. He constantly pretends he's been shot in a movie whenever he is hurt to deter from the realism of the war, and though he recognises how damaging this is, he continues to do it for his own protection.- So much is said about class, especially when talking about the guy who used his suitcases, and the visions he has of Sally drinking tea with the Lunts and the Ivy League boys. He hates it, although he is part of that culture himself.- Holden really doesn't like Romeo and Juliet because they did dumb shit when they tried to grow up too fast.- Harris Macklin is the opposite to Stradlater.- Holden takes Sally's hand when he wants to run away with her because he used to do that with Jane, and he's trying to reclaim those feelings- He sees no ducks in the park when he finally gets there because every other duck has flown away apart from him, leaving him in his own bubble of self denial and isolation.- There is a link between when James Castle commits suicide and when Holden contemplates suicide at the hotel. Holden is worried that nobody will cover up his body like Antolini did for James because he doesn't believe that the adults around him are capable of the good that Antolini is. - The 'fall' that Antolini talks about to Holden is the fall into adulthood.- Holden dreams of going into an isolated cabin to live his perfect life which is weirdly the exact same thing that Salinger did.- The mention of the 'psychoanalyst' at the end convinces me that Holden is in fact an a mental institution rather than being treated at a hospital for TB, which is what many speculate. He states that he 'practically' got TB, not that he actually got it, and we would be wise to distrust Holden when he uses such slippery language.",

        "Alright.I've read quite a few books in my lifetime thus far; enough to believe that I have a hold on what constitutes as good literature and what does not. Books are one of the greatest passions in my life, and I have utmost respect for authors. I never, ever want to hate a piece of literature, especially one that has had such a tremendous impact on the greater culture.However. I can't help it here. And I can honestAlright.I've read quite a few books in my lifetime thus far; enough to believe that I have a hold on what constitutes as good literature and what does not. Books are one of the greatest passions in my life, and I have utmost respect for authors. I never, ever want to hate a piece of literature, especially one that has had such a tremendous impact on the greater culture.However. I can't help it here. The Catcher in the Rye is one of the worst books I have ever picked up and read. And I can honestly say that I did genuinely read it, cover to cover. Its popularity and praise baffles me to no end, and the fact that so many angsty adolescents swear to identify with Holden is even  baffling. Holden whines for two-hundred pages about life, love, and his abhorrence for fake, conformist idiots. It blows my mind how three-quarters of the readers who claim to be Holden's kindred spirit are those exact idiots he just spent an entire twenty-six chapters detesting.The plot of the story...wait, what plot? The entire book is one giant, boring, continuous narrative that circles around endlessly in Caufield's head. Let's put it this way. If you were feeling particularly pretentious and moody one day, and you went on a walk to clear your head, the spiteful, mouthy ramblings of your conscience would be on par with what this book is essentially all about. To be blunt, Salinger; no one cares. If I wanted to read two-hundred pages of some over-priveleged youth complaining about anything and everything for no legitimate reason, I'd pick up my old high school diary. I have found that most of the people who truly enjoy this book aren't die-hard readers. Now, that may be a completely personal experience, and if it is, I do apologize. My best friend, who is just as much if not  of a bookworm than I am, does nothing but close her eyes and shake her head in disappointment at the mention of The Catcher in the Rye.Read this book at your own risk. Chances are, you will either fall completely in love with it or you will hate it with a fiery passion. I was of the latter, quite obviously. I simply did not, do not get it.",

        "I view The Catcher in the Rye as a difficult and complicated book to review. Not because it's a difficult or overly complicated book; it's not. But, because this book is read through so many different lenses, taught by so many different types of teachers in school and interpreted in so many different types of ways, it has become an object of visceral reaction (or something people merely scratch their head about wondering what all the fuss is about). Simply put, this is one of the best selling noI view The Catcher in the Rye as a difficult and complicated book to review. Not because it's a difficult or overly complicated book; it's not. But, because this book is read through so many different lenses, taught by so many different types of teachers in school and interpreted in so many different types of ways, it has become an object of visceral reaction (or something people merely scratch their head about wondering what all the fuss is about). Simply put, this is one of the best selling novels of all time and has remained a hot commodity (averaging about 250,000 copies a year). With so many people reading this book, with so much academic emphasis placed upon it, and with persistent conversation about the book over time, it has simply become difficult to establish order in the chaos. Some people go so far as to consider this book a brand, and will try and categorize (one way or the other) people based on whether or not someone likes this book. So what's the deal? Well let's explore a couple of the different ways someone might \"get something\" out of this novel:It seems the predominant view of this book is that it is essentially a novel of teen angst. That it deals with the issues of having to grow up, give up your childhood, and find our place in the big, scary, uncertain world. This view might also claim the book deals with issues of belonging, alienation, and some identity issues. From what I've seen, people who subscribe to this interpretation of the novel are also likely to claim that they identify with Holden Caulfield (the main character from whose point of view the book is written). That they get him, or perhaps  accurately, that Holden Caulfield gets the reader. Some people who do not hold this book in high regard often arrive at the same interpretation, but take the opposite position. They claim that Holden Caulfield is a whiny brat. That he needs to just get over his problems, grow up, and stop being so annoying. Now, I'm not particularly qualified to tell someone what a book is or is not about, and if someone wants claim that one of the above descriptions is an accurate portrayal of the book, then I can't exactly tell them that they're wrong. But I sort of think it. Perhaps not so much that they're wrong, but that they aren't entirely accurate. You see, I believe it's a drastic error to think that this book was written for the reader. I think this book isn't written for us, it's written for J.D. Salinger, and it just happens to be published so we can read it. What I mean by that, is that the author of this book is inseparable from the novel itself. J.D. Salinger was drafted into the army for World War 2, and fought in Europe, from Normandy to Berlin until the end of the war. He participated in D-Day, The battle of the Bulge, Bastogne, and countless other horrific engagements. He was also one of the first Americans to enter and liberate a Nazi concentration camp. He told his daughter once that \"You never really get the smell of burning flesh out of your nose entirely, no matter how long you live.\" It's no large stretch of anyone's imagination to say that J.D. Salinger was suffering, deeply, from PTSD. So let's look back on Holden Caulfield. A teenager who seems unable to move on with his life. He saw (view spoiler)[a classmate commit a violent suicide while wearing an article of Holden's clothing (hide spoiler)]. And let's not forget Holden's most important source of trauma (view spoiler)[his little brother Allie, whose death he blames himself for (hide spoiler)]. The big sort of \"reveal\" at the end is that (view spoiler)[Holden is telling the whole story to a doctor within the walls of a mental institution (hide spoiler)]. Does Holden Caulfield behave the way he does in the novel because he's some angsty teen who feels uncertain about his future, or does he behave the way he does because he's a teenager suffering from Post-Traumatic Stress? After The Catcher in the Rye was published, J.D. Salinger quickly became a recluse and withdrew from public life. I can imagine why. He writes a novel that is the manifestation of his way of trying to work through unimaginable trauma, and in return he gets middle-class teenagers saying, \"That's just like me! I know what he's going through!\" That may be true for a very small percentage of readers, but for most of us we simply don't. Now, if someone wants to like the book because it \"speaks\" to them, or because it deals with some issues that they can connect with, then that's great! I'll almost always applaud when someone finds a new book they love, and I'm not trying to dismiss other people who either like or do not like this novel.I simply think it's particularly important to keep context in mind when talking (or disagreeing) about this book. I obviously do have a higher opinion of this novel, since I rated it 5-stars, but I have a high opinion of the novel because it reveals to me some interesting ways in how the human mind works. I find I can respect Salinger for writing a book that was so personal to him, and I enjoy romping through a true war-veteran's portrayal of a boy who lost his innocence and is trying to make sense of the world and people around him. Holden's narrative is often flavorless, his descriptions minute, unfair, and childish. Yet, that's the point. Holden focuses on the unimportant things about people and life, gives them a meaning that is blown out of proportion, and snags himself on these small details. Why? Because it's a coping mechanism that prevents him from dealing with the things that truly matter. He uses the term phony throughout the book. Why is everything phony? Because reality is too painful. Bottom line? I enjoy this book. I think there's a surprising amount of meat to chew on, for it being such a quick read. Some people might say, \"Oh, there's THAT guy. He's one of those who like The Catcher in the Rye. He's one of THOSE types of people.\" But frankly, that's fine. I don't like this book because I'm trying to make some emotional statement. I just simply like it. Is it the best book ever? Of course not. But I dig it for what it is, and I think everyone should give it a fair chance. ",
        "I just finished reading Nine Stories and had no intention of reading  Salinger right away. But I was surprised at how cute and clever I found his writing after all these years.
