Maybe Salinger wouldn‚Äôt have become so famous if he hadn‚Äôt been so infamous. Maybe it was because he said some things about mothers that weren‚Äôt so kind. Right that minute, the conductor came around for old Mrs. Morrow‚Äôs ticket, and it gave me a chance to quit shooting it. I‚Äôm glad I shot it for a while, though. You take a guy like Morrow who‚Äôs always snapping their towel at people‚Äôs asses ‚Äì really trying to hurt somebody with it ‚Äì they don‚Äôt just stay a rat while they‚Äôre a kid. They stay a rat their whole life. But I‚Äôll bet, after all the crap I shot, Mrs. Morrow‚Äôll keep thinking of him now as this very shy, modest guy that wouldn‚Äôt let us nominate him for president. She might. You can‚Äôt tell. Mothers aren‚Äôt too sharp about that stuff.  I mean, Holden was really good at shooting it and could have gotten Salinger into a lot of unnecessary trouble. In fact, I guess he did. He called Morrow a ‚Äúsonuvabitch‚Äù when the guy‚Äôs mother wasn‚Äôt around. Salinger didn‚Äôt invent trash talk, but Holden, well ...And, of course, young sister Phoebe challenged Holden to think of something he liked and Holden imagined the scene we all associate with this book: ‚ÄúAnyway, I keep picturing all these little kids playing some game in this big field of rye and all. Thousands of little kids, and nobody‚Äôs around ‚Äì nobody big, I mean ‚Äì except me. And I‚Äôm standing on the edge of some crazy cliff. What I have to do, I have to catch everybody if they start to go over the cliff ‚Äì I mean if they‚Äôre running and they don‚Äôt look where they‚Äôre going I have to come out from somewhere and catch them. That‚Äôs all I‚Äôd do all day. I‚Äôd just be the catcher in the rye and all. I know it‚Äôs crazy, but that‚Äôs the only thing I‚Äôd really like to be. I know it‚Äôs crazy.‚Äù Is recapturing the moment when you first read that paragraph years ago, worth reading the entire book again? At that very moment it seemed like it was worth it. Five stars. That one extra star is for that moment. I guess that is serious nostalgia.Now on to read one  book that dissects Salinger based on too little information. The man was a mystery. ",
        "Teen angst is a glorious thing. I can remember going through that part of my life and I cringe looking back at certain moments when I let those emotions/feelings dictate something I said or did. It‚Äôs not that they don‚Äôt hold value but that the person is acting upon them immediately and in an over the top way. As you get older you learn to blunt the sharp edges of those feelings and let them simmer  before acting upon them. You also learn to vent better by talking them out or writing them dowTeen angst is a glorious thing. I can remember going through that part of my life and I cringe looking back at certain moments when I let those emotions/feelings dictate something I said or did. It‚Äôs not that they don‚Äôt hold value but that the person is acting upon them immediately and in an over the top way. As you get older you learn to blunt the sharp edges of those feelings and let them simmer  before acting upon them. You also learn to vent better by talking them out or writing them down (to name a few), so you can see the root of the issue and understand why you are reacting the way you are.This is the story of a boy that is letting every emotion and feeling he has immediately guide every decision he makes. If school sucks you quit, if your family doesn‚Äôt understand you run away, if your friend goes on a date with the girl you like you pick a fight with him. He isn‚Äôt allowing any time to review those feelings and to understand better why he is feeling them. Everything he feels is unique to only him and everything he does is in the moment. I completely understand that this is common behavior for that age range (I was guilty of it myself) and I am not knocking Holden for it. I‚Äôm sure I will look back in twenty years at my current self and cringe for a whole new set of reasons with the midlife crisis phase of my life being right around the corner. From the reader‚Äôs perspective you are inside of Holden‚Äôs head listening to his side of it all and at first it is quite an interesting voice. It doesn‚Äôt take long for that interest to wane and for his voice to drift into nails on a chalkboard territory. It‚Äôs like whatever made the writing style unique and amazing at first didn‚Äôt last throughout the rest of the book. A few chapters in and I could have started skipping paragraphs and/or chapters and I would not have lost the plot at all. It was just him internally rationalizing his self-destructive behavior and not understanding why the results of that behavior was happening to him. You can like a book for different reasons such as writing style/voice, characters, story and how it made you feel, and I could not check any of those boxes for this book.",
        "Depressed, disoriented, displaced, and later, you learn that he is also traumatized. So goes Salinger's teenaged protagonist, Holden. When he tells you in the beginning that he doesn't want to talk about his \"lousy childhood\" and how his \"parents were occupied\" before they had him, be thankful that he told you at least that because Holden doesn't seem to like talking about what's bothering him. He will, however, tell you about everyone else. It helps that at first glance, Holden commands your atDepressed, disoriented, displaced, and later, you learn that he is also traumatized. So goes Salinger's teenaged protagonist, Holden. When he tells you in the beginning that he doesn't want to talk about his \"lousy childhood\" and how his \"parents were occupied\" before they had him, be thankful that he told you at least that because Holden doesn't seem to like talking about what's bothering him. He will, however, tell you about everyone else. It helps that at first glance, Holden commands your attention. This is where Salinger's mastery of character-voice is spellbinding, he seems to enjoy toying with stream of consciousness, seamlessly veering between first and second point of views, to create fiction in opposition of boarding schools, much like Orwell did in his nonfiction essay, Such, Such Were The Joys.Holden is disappointed in human behavior: You should've seen the way he did with my roommate's parents. I mean if a boy's mother was sort of fat or corny-looking or something, and if somebody's father was one of those guys that wear those suits with very big shoulders and corny black-and-white shoes, then old Haas would just shake hands with them and give them a phony smile and then he'd go talk, for maybe a half an hour, with somebody else's parents. I can't stand that stuff. It drives me crazy. It makes me so depressed.From there on, we enter Holden's depression. And you see why the protagonist is a young boy. The rant would not have worked for an adult because, as we know, the teenage mind is an enigma. Yes, teenagers can get away with such rueful raves. This teenager is the son of absent parents: a hard-working but distant lawyer-father, and a semi-reclusive mother. His older brother is a famous writer, his other brother is deceased (and you can tell that this really bothers him) and he simply adores his little sister, Phoebe. So what's the problem? Well, you don't get snippets until later in the book. But back to Holden's depression, because he makes you aware of it throughout the book. This is one instance where showing versus telling in literary fiction, is done with equal measure. Eighty pages in: Holden is too depressed to care whether he had a good view or not in his hotel room (you heard correctly, this teeanager manages to purchase booze and hotel rooms and all). Ninety eight pages in: Radio City Music Hall makes Holden so depressed, he just can't stand it. One hundred and six pages in: \"New York's terrible when somebody laughs on the street very late at night. You can hear it for miles. It makes you feel so lonesome and depressed.\"Lots of opinions, that Holden. He is a virgin who somehow has procured knowledge about women. And just because he doesn't do his schoolwork, don't think that he doesn't read. In fact, he will tell you how considers A Farewell to Arms a phony book, but he is crazy about The Great Gatsby .Almost two hundred pages in, and I thought something was going to happen. Really, I did. Phoebe intervenes and you really start to see Holden because through their dialogue, she's telling us some things. Afterwards, when his former teacher, Mr. Antolini intervenes, my hopes are really up because maybe this is turning into a lesson after all, especially since the teacher gives Holden the \"many, many men have been just as troubled morally and spiritually as you are right now...you'll learn from them\" speech. Then boom, my hopes are smashed when Holden thinks Mr. Antolini is being--well, you know. The trauma from seeing another kid die in such a horrific way, plus the trauma that comes from people always being \"perverty\" around him in boarding school, makes you understand why Holden is so depressed. But the idea that there is not enough of a setup to allow you to really see him clearly (in fact it's easy to miss the important details) is also depressing. Not to mention the fact that there is no silver lining at the end.Sigh. Now I'm a bit depressed. *3.5 stars*",

        "The Catcher in the Rye often evokes a passionate reaction when mentioned. Some people feel it fails to meet the expectations generated by the mystique it is shrouded in or they dislike the whining, self-obsessed protagonist. Others love everything about it. I fall into that latter category. However you feel about it, there is little doubt that The Catcher in the Rye effectively captures the reality of being a teenager with haunting accuracy and articulates so well what it feels like to be that aThe Catcher in the Rye often evokes a passionate reaction when mentioned. Some people feel it fails to meet the expectations generated by the mystique it is shrouded in or they dislike the whining, self-obsessed protagonist. Others love everything about it. I fall into that latter category. However you feel about it, there is little doubt that The Catcher in the Rye effectively captures the reality of being a teenager with haunting accuracy and articulates so well what it feels like to be that awkward age between childhood and adulthood. It was published at a time when young adult fiction didn't really exist, so that in itself spoke to teenagers at that time. It also had - and continues to have - mass appeal: published in the lead up to the Civil Rights Movement, Holden became an icon of teenage rebellion for disaffected youth, and throughout history (even in the wake of Trump's Presidential election) young people have turned to the novel for understanding. Part of the beauty and the popularity of this book is that Holden's teenage angst speaks to everybody. It has been called \"The Great American Novel\" but I think it should be called \"The Great Teenage Novel\", because while not all of us are American, all of us have been teenagers. The Catcher in the Rye opens with seventeen-year-old Holden Caulfield residing in an unspecified institution in Southern California in either 1949 or 1950. The story unfolds as Holden recalls the events of the previous Christmas, after he was expelled from Pencey Preparatory Academy, a boarding school in Pennsylvania, owing to his unsatisfactory academic performance. The novels moves on to follow Holden on his perambulations around New York city that December as he attempts to find those few genuine people and true human connection.The novel was originally intended by Salinger for an adult readership, but Holden‚Äôs intense personal tale of his experiences amid the 'phoneys' of society has appealed to generations of adolescents ever since. Holden's stream of consciousness first-person narrative follows his exact thought processes so makes for a very intimate reading that not only speaks directly to the heart but to every generation of teenager, affirming that frustrated adolescents are not alone.The Catcher in the Rye addresses themes of identity, belonging, loss, connection, parental ignorance and sex, but most notably it is a story about the protection of innocence, especially of children, and the struggle of growing up; of that often painful transition from the comfortable and protected chrysalis of childhood to the unfamiliar and daunting reality of adolescence. I adore this book: it was one of the first novels to really speak to me as a young teenager and it will always have a very special place in my heart.",
        "The Catcher in the Rye is a coming of age kind of story, set around the 1950s in New York. The story is narrated from the perspective of our main character Holden Caulfield, who is telling us the story from a psychiatric facility a few months after the events of the novel. The first thing I noticed when I started reading The Catcher in the Rye was the cynicism and bitterness of Holden's voice. At first it kind of got on my nerves because Holden is just too pessimistic about life and people arounThe Catcher in the Rye is a coming of age kind of story, set around the 1950s in New York. The story is narrated from the perspective of our main character Holden Caulfield, who is telling us the story from a psychiatric facility a few months after the events of the novel. The first thing I noticed when I started reading The Catcher in the Rye was the cynicism and bitterness of Holden's voice. At first it kind of got on my nerves because Holden is just too pessimistic about life and people around him. But then I started to understand that The Catcher in the Rye was nothing  than a chronicle of Holden Caulfield's emotional breakdown and I started to sympathize with him.Now we have this whole \"Phoniness\" situation, which is probably the most famous phrase in the novel. It is one of Holden's favorite concepts. He uses it many times through out the novel in order to describe the hypocrisy, superficiality and pretension that he encounters in the world around him. Holden explains that all the adults are by definition phonies, and the worst part is that they can't even see their phoniness. Phoniness, for Holden stands as an emblem of everything that's wrong in the world, which provides him with an excuse to withdraw into his cynical isolation.Holden's loneliness is a  concrete manifestation of his alienation issues, a big part of the novel describes his almost manic quest for companionship as he flies from one meaningless encounter to the other. As a reader we understand pretty soon in the novel that Holden uses his alienation as form of self preservation, and we also easily link his unusual behavior to the death of his younger brother Allie. Even though Holden never explicitly admit it, this event has obviously affected him a great deal.The \"Catcher in the Rye\" as a source of the title of the book deserves a close inspection. When Phoebe asks her brother what he wants to do in the future he answers her with this image from the song \"catcher in the rye\" Holden wants to protect the kids playing in the rye field from falling over the cliff by \"catching\" them, in other words Holden wants to protect kids from falling out of innocence into the adult world including the knowledge of sex. The thing is that Holden is mistaken in the lyrics of the song which are \"if a body meet a body coming in the rye\" the song asks if it's wrong for two people to have a romantic encounter out in the field, which is the exact opposite of what Holden wants the song to mean.Over all I really enjoyed The Catcher in the Rye and I will definitely be rereading it in the future, I already miss Holden and his famous red hat of individuality.         "‚ÄúAmong other things, you'll find that you're not the first person who was ever confused and frightened and even sickened by human behavior. You're by no means alone on that score, you'll be excited and stimulated to know. Many, many men have been just as troubled morally and spiritually as you are right now. Happily, some of them kept records of their troubles. You'll learn from them‚Äîif you want to. Just as someday, if you have something to offer, someone will learn something from you. It's a be‚ÄúAmong other things, you'll find that you're not the first person who was ever confused and frightened and even sickened by human behavior. You're by no means alone on that score, you'll be excited and stimulated to know. Many, many men have been just as troubled morally and spiritually as you are right now. Happily, some of them kept records of their troubles. You'll learn from them‚Äîif you want to. Just as someday, if you have something to offer, someone will learn something from you. It's a beautiful reciprocal arrangement. And it isn't education. It's history. It's poetry.‚ÄùSalinger‚Äôs ‚ÄòA Catcher in the Rye‚Äô is a classic coming of age novel that is entirely made up of teenage angst, raunchy observations and a lingering feeling of loss (of childhood innocence). Holden Caulfield, currently in a sanatorium undergoing treatment for his mental condition is revisiting his 16-year-old self, who has been expelled from school after failing almost all of his exams. As he travels back to New York, where his family resides, he elaborately describes the two days succeeding his expulsion. After bidding a brief farewell to his history teacher and a fight with his roommate, Holden boards a train to Manhattan with hopes to stay in a hotel. He then experiences a whole different world through his hotel room. Men and women involved in strange acts of arousal, a man dressing like a woman, events that arouse Holden but also piques his curiosity about this bit of the world that is secretive and forbidden. The same night he tries to persuade a stripper to sleep with him but without any success. He then moves on to flirting with random women at the bar. There is also a flashback to how Holden met Jane Gallagher, a girl he once dated and still admires, giving us glimpses of a care free and love struck Holden, very different from the present one. As the story moves forward, we see Holden trying to hold on tight to the good memories while simultaneously trying to fit in. Despite the lack of an obvious plot, Holden‚Äôs blatant observation and subsequent narration conveys the story well. The story ends abruptly, but also with a hopeful note giving the impression of a conflicted teenager finally getting a hang on how it all works.The reason for the title is hiding in plain sight and as the pieces of the puzzle keep coming, it starts to make sense. It is easy to empathize with Holden but what‚Äôs easier is to see yourself in him, especially if you are a 16-year-old, carrying the burden of responsibilities and are overwhelmed with the way things are progressing. For my adult self, this story is a trip back to memory lane.‚ÄòThe Catcher in the Rye‚Äô faced a ban at multiple schools and universities in the US of A because of an abundance in cuss words. Holden is honest with his feeling and his vernacular is that of a typical teenager trying to make sense of the responsibilities put on him, those that were irrelevant a few days back. He is frustrated, holding on to the feeling of freedom one experiences as a child. He wants to bawl with joy when he sees his sister Phoebe enjoy the ride in the park because he believes he is succeeding in preserving her childlike state. Holden is taking the big, scary plunge into teenage-hood and is visibly scared. He discards traditional values and refers to adults as ‚Äòphonies‚Äô. He is rash with his actions and faces a mental breakdown every now and then.The story is also sexually explicit in places and contains multiple references to premarital sex and prostitution, which, given the year of its publication, was a taboo and was considered obscene and out of place. There‚Äôs also alcoholism, piercing remarks on God, women and people with disabilities.Right from the beginning, it‚Äôs the humor that keeps the readers gripped and it is this same humor that makes the story monotonous after a certain time. Sarcasm and irony are recurring themes and gives an impression that Holden is using it as a shield as he fails to come to terms with his own experience in the real world. Holden challenges the hypocrisy in his typical rash and ignorant ways and is brutal with his words (so much that he disrupts his relationship with his friends).In retrospect, Holden is also deeply affected by the death of his elder brother and misses his presence. He might be in need of his brother to hold his hand and guide him through this transition, but that is purely speculative. Holden in trying to fit in, to shoulder the responsibilities put on him. He is in need of a deep and meaningful connection, but his trauma prevents him from opening up. He, however, never gets violent. It‚Äôs just his tongue, relentlessly critical of himself, hurling abuses and voicing insecurities in an anxious, worrisome way.‚ÄòThe Catcher in the Rye‚Äô, despite the mixed opinions, has inspired a lot of people from every walk of life with its honest narration and a protagonist who is eerily similar to most of our teenage selves. It is a classic that must be on you TBR if you‚Äôre fond of the genre. It talks about new beginnings after a particularly dreadful episode of existential crisis and gives up hope, to keep going, despite the hardships.",

I went to a French-speaking high school about a hundred kilometers west of Montr√©al. At the time, everyone was listening to U2 and Tears for Fears and The Cure and only the anglophone minority of which I was a part actually understood the words to those songs. That used to kill me. You‚Äôd have a high school dance and everyone would be kind of surf-dancing to ‚ÄúBoys don‚Äôt cry‚Äù and most of them had no idea what the song was about, but it didn‚Äôt keep them from adopting that pouting stance with their front hair hanging over the left side of their downcast faces.Claudia had that look too. When a book is this praised and also notorious(?) it's difficult to gauge my personal reflections.I sympathised a lot with Holden. Almost every page is dripping with boredom, directionlessness and alienation. Particular how uncomfortable it makes others when he wants them to listen to his feelings: even fine well knowing he might not mean what he says even a few minutes later, no one can take it. He is passive aggressively ‚Äútrained‚Äù into either saying nothing or exactly what someone else wants to hear. The only times he‚Äôs allowed to speak his mind at length are when he elaborates upon what he knows is exactly what the other person wants to hear, even arguing in one case, while lying to a mother about her son on the train, that she would lack so much perspective on her own son as to believe Holden‚Äôs lies forever. It‚Äôs no wonder Holden is unable to interpret his actions with no one to talk to.Post-war New York is a place in time that likely fully embraced comfort, safety, routine, and banal pleasantry, until, in Holden‚Äôs eyes, citizens became babyish phonies incapable of real interaction. The part at the end about Holden‚Äôs little sister seems to be about the perpetual production of innocence and those who will always question trivial societal structures, etiquettes and habits. Holden spends most of his time talking to adults and those who idolize adults (actors and the upper class), and given that this novel was originally intended for an adult audience, do we see Holden as representative of the coming generation to highlight the responsibilities of the adult generation to the next one down, and the harm of all their fearful fakery? Holden opts for the possibility offered by children over the rigid formalities of adults: he hopes for change.I‚Äôve been a proper out-of-uni adult for about 2 years now and heavily steeped in all the banal status-and-banter constructs of the brainless, so I get Holden‚Äôs fears about what will happen to him when he grows up. Why wouldn‚Äôt they come true? I don‚Äôt know.I can also see why this is popular amongst alienated teen folk but I think a lot of the insights had to have been made by an adult for an adult. (Likely an English teacher will find this in two years and write back to me as if I just wrote this, at which point I‚Äôll probably think something else! Also I‚Äôm really happy for you but Iain Banks penned the best teen angst stuff of all time!) Holden is an unreliable narrator who cannot fully articulate what his confessions are doing, which I believe is to draw together a collective mentality of reaction to the zeitgeist. He is prone to surface observations and hyperbole. He feels often that his problems are his and his alone and that nobody gets him and everyone else is fake. Loner ‚Äúkillers‚Äù perhaps attach themselves to his view as truth: ‚ÄúMe and you, Holden, me and you, and no one else!‚Äù whereas the book‚Äôs popularity is testament to many people feeling like Holden. We can be many people feeling like the only ones, but we can‚Äôt all be the only ones. To say that his angst is just a phase is kinda stupid. It may be aggravated by teenage years but it is brought about by the environment rather than Holden himself who is not inarticulate nor unintelligent. That environment still exists today. Maybe always will‚Ä¶Anyways, I consider myself an open guy who‚Äôs willing to discuss anything with anyone. But I‚Äôve also perpetuated the illusion of society‚Äôs wall of artifice: say someone I don‚Äôt know very well skips a few steps in the normal social constructs of getting to know me and stands too close and does the ‚ÄúDon‚Äôt you just think that everyone‚Äù etcetera, you know the type, well, I‚Äôll sigh and bat them away because their thoughts to me will be shallow and repetitious and maybe I was having a good time and don‚Äôt wanna get into it, whereas this guy will walk away thinking that his attitude has been confirmed, that he‚Äôs him and I‚Äôm one of them. Maybe I was tired. Maybe I‚Äôd had that same conversation recently with someone else and didn‚Äôt feel like having it again so soon. Maybe I was really tired. Maybe if I had  time and I knew the guy better I‚Äôd listen. Or if I thought he‚Äôd read up about his ideas even in the slightest so he could be a bit objective. I can forgive myself for this every now and then: after all, I‚Äôm only human and as much as I believe in the curative properties of empathy and compassion, I know them to be very slowly-renewable resources that are best conserved. Holden Caulfeild is struggling with just this problem. When the story first begins, he has just been kicked out of another school. Finding himself alone on a friday night Holden gets nervous becuase his roomate is on a date with a childhood friend of his. He can't help himself but wonder what his roomate and Jane are doing. Getting agitated by that and his dormatory roomate he decides to go to New York Manhatten for the weekend. Once on the train he meets a lady, a mother of a classmate. He despises the student, but he can't help himself but to lie to her. Once off the train he goes to a motel and watches people from his widow. Next, he calls a lady that is said to be \"easy\". But basically, all I knew about the book was that the main character is called Holden Caulfield, and it's J.D. Salinger's only novel.And I kind of wish I'd left it that way. I was on the struggle bus with this book essentially from the get go. The opening few chapters - the stuff before Holden leaves school - reminded me a lot of Dead Poets Society, and  specifically Charlie. Seeing as Dead Poets Society is one of my favourite movies, I was moderately interested in the story.But then Holden left school, and things went rapidly downhill. We're trapped in Holden's head for endless pages with few other characters to break things up. And this was hard for me, because I didn't like Holden AT ALL. He's full of rich white boy problems, where everything is someone else's fault. Holden always thinks he's in the right, despite however much evidence is stacked up against him. Essentially, I couldn't stand him or the narrative style.I REALLY didn't like the writing. Admittedly, it's a product of the time. It's set in the late 1940s and is told by a teenage boy, so the amount of misogyny that comes through in the story is almost to be expected. But the amount of repetition in the writing drove me up the wall. Holden will make an observation about another character, then insert an anecdote about that character doing the thing he observed them doing, then repeat his observation. Like, \"The girl could dance. [Insert long winded anecdote about said girl dancing here] I tell you, the girl could dance.\"YES. WE KNOW. YOU LITERALLY JUST TOLD US.By the end of the book, I just flat out didn't care, and when it featured a total non-ending on top of all the repetition and angst and misogyny that I'd already had to wade through, I was tempted to throw the book across the room.I feel like this is a book that's  enjoyable if a) you study it in school and can unpackage all the things that Salinger is trying to say, or b) you're an angsty teenager when you read it. So maybe if I'd read this as a teenager, I'd see the appeal. But as an adult? NOPE.",

What he didn‚Äôt know was the reaction or strings of actions would be created by this book.Mark David Chapman shot John Lennon and had The Catcher in the Rye in his hand. John Hinckley Jr. attempted an assassination on Ronald Reagan in 1981 and one of the books owned by him was the one written by Salinger. There are several movie and television references to the novel as well. What is it about this book that evokes such reactions? Why? To my sixteen-year old mind, unwell and in bed, it was just another novel lent to me by my uncle and I had to read it. I read it. I loved it and that was it.The Catcher in the Rye is not just another novel then. It is the voice of several generations of teenagers in the sense of the world. It is the world of angst and no sense of direction. Or maybe it is the voice of intellectualizing everything or trivializing it all. Holden Caulfield is  than an icon. He is someone who is trying to make sense of his life and life around him. It might appear to be as simple as this, when it is not or may be it is. He encounters people ‚Äì different people as he takes off from his fancy school Pencey Prep and takes on his journey in New York City. This is where it all begins or almost.The book was banned in most schools in the US of A. It is because of its vulgar language, which honestly I did not have a problem with then or now. To me the writing is just surreal, even after rereading it after fourteen years. It just manages to evoke the same sentiments in me and that is why I call it timeless. It talks about adolescence and its struggle like no other book. The Catcher in the Rye in that sense of the word is truly a classic and will be for years to come. I am glad I reread it. Thanks to my The Novel Cure Reading Challenge. It is guaranteed to cure angst of adolescence.",

Holden is clearly suffering from anxiety and terrible depression, and at the age of sixteen, has been thrown out of three schools. Despite this, he is funny, caring, intelligent and insightful, and as far as he is concerned the only people who are not \"phoney\" are children. Holden likes kids, and he's good with them, he is especially close to his ten year old sister Phoebe. Throughout his story Holden mentions many times how depressed he is and a few times he states that he wishes he was dead. He has suffered great loss and trauma; the death of his beloved brother Allie. Holden also witnessed the suicide of a classmate and had reason to believe that his favorite teacher tried to molest him. As I read about Holden on his journey around New York city, I sensed that he was becoming  anxious, so anxious that he experienced two panic attacks, during one he passed out. A big part of Holden's problem is his fear of growing up, and a longing for his childhood. He hates change, and desperately wants things to stay the same, this is why he loved the museum and the carousel. Things in the museum had stayed the same for decades, and Holden went there as a child, his life was happier when he was a kid, when Allie was alive. That's one nice thing about carrousels, they always play the same songs.\" While watching Phoebe on the carrousel ride, Holden tells us how happy he feels.         "I was between \"liked it\" and \"really liked it\" on this, so I went ahead and erred on the side of enthusiasm.Two things were striking to me, this time around. First, this is a *very* different book to a woman in her forties than it was to a teenager. Oddly, in some ways I felt I could relate to it  now. This passage, for instance:\"I took her dress over to the closet and hung it up for her. It was funny. It made me feel sort of sad when I hung it up. I thought of her going in a store and buyinI was between \"liked it\" and \"really liked it\" on this, so I went ahead and erred on the side of enthusiasm.Two things were striking to me, this time around. First, this is a *very* different book to a woman in her forties than it was to a teenager. Oddly, in some ways I felt I could relate to it  now. This passage, for instance:\"I took her dress over to the closet and hung it up for her. It was funny. It made me feel sort of sad when I hung it up. I thought of her going in a store and buying it, and nobody in the store knowing she was a prostitute and all. The salesman probably just thought she was a regular girl when she bought it. It made me feel sad as hell -- I don't know why exactly.\"That's the sort of weird moment I have all the time. Not that I do a lot of hanging out with prostitutes, mind you. Those days are long over. But the point is, I tend to make odd connections and think strange little thoughts like that.However, one thing I *wasn't* in a position to evaluate as a young and very dense reader was how whiny Holden can come across as. I love the scene where his younger sister Phoebe calls him out on this: \"You don't like *any*thing that's happening.\"It made me even  depressed when she said that.\"Yes I do. Yes I do. *Sure* I do. Don't say that. Why the hell do you say that?\"\"Because you don't. You don't like any schools. You don't like a million things. You *don't.*\"By this point in the book, it feels pretty true. Holden's observations can be funny and canny and even just beautiful, but past a certain point, it's hard not to want to say, \"Yes, okay, you're special and unique and a darned adorable snowflake. Now: enough with the rich-kid angst, already!\" Which is not the kind of thing I would have thought as a teenager, because I was too busy being full of angst. Okay, and trying to survive being a *real* runaway, which is rather different from fantasizing. Especially if you're female and broke rather than male and privileged. But I digress.The other reason this was such a different read several decades since the last time was that the last time I read \"Catcher,\" I hadn't just seen the trailer for a documentary about Salinger. I certainly wasn't aware that several men who adored \"Catcher\" had committed assassinations, or attempted assassinations. But that was almost all I could think about when I read it this time.And let me tell you -- reading \"Catcher\" while keeping a close eye out for the kind of weirdness a certain kind of crazy might latch onto is a very disturbing experience. Please note that even if I weren't attempting to write for a living myself, I would have very little patience with the idea of a work of art being hauled into court (a court of opinion, at least, if not of law) as both motive and weapon. And even if a book were written and published with the express intention of prompting citizens to take lives, responsibility for resulting action falls on those who commit murder. That said? Reading \"Catcher\" with all this in mind freaked me out. Because this really is a story of madness. Holden isn't some sweet, strange little puppy who's too sensitive for this cruel world. He is exactly what he frequently, jokingly refers to himself as: a madman. He refers quite lightly to shooting people. He has frequent fantasies of bloody violence, committed against both himself and others. He cares very little for most other people, calculating only what he can get from them. He seeks out boys and men he despises simply because he's lonely or bored or needs somewhere to go. He avoids the one admirable girl he knows, making moves on physically attractive but hateable women instead. (But who does he despise most of all? \"Phonies.\") Even when something makes him laugh, how does Holden describe it? Does he ever say something is funny or amusing? No. \"That kills me.\" \"That just about killed me.\" He really is murder or suicide, or both, waiting to happen.So why don't I hate this book?Well, it's brilliant. Also, I really *do* relate to some of the gems of perspective Holden has to offer. When his roommate asks him to write a composition for him, he cautions Holden not to write it *too* well, or the teacher will catch on. \"So I mean don't stick all the commas and stuff in the right place.\"Hoo, boy -- Holden starts railing on that, and brother he was preaching to the *choir.* People really *do* denigrate writing talent by boiling it down to the comparatively trivial details. People do that with *everything.* For instance: I worked for years with kids -- as a babysitter, nanny, teacher's assistant, tutor, and the dismissively named position of \"program aide\" in a home for severely disabled children, where I did everything from occupational therapy to stomach-tube feedings. I worked my arse off, is what I'm saying. And then one day my older sister, who was an actress at the time, talked about some after-school work she was doing with a fourth-grade class, and how she was having a hard time communicating with the children. \"I just don't have that magic *thing* you have with kids,\" she said.Great. Here *I* thought it was a combination of backbreaking labor, constant application, studying, figuring out what worked and throwing out what didn't, and praying for patience when I was fresh out. Turns out, I shouldn't have tried so hard! I could have just fallen back on my magic thing!Anyway. Reading this book again after so many years was a strange, often dark but ultimately beautiful journey. My friends who admire Salinger tell me this is *not* his best work, so I have \"Franny and Zooey\" lined up next. ",

I picked up on so much subtext and hints about the underlying story that I never picked up on before. Sure, I understood that it was primarily about a 16-year-old frustrated by the loss of innocence, but I now see so much  that I never paid attention to previously -- his depression/suicidal ideation, trauma surrounding his brother's death, a complicated relationship with sex, and hints dropped throughout the book about sexual abuse and pedophilia towards Holden (and even one instance regarding a peer at one of his previous schools that completely went over my head the first time I read this). This book is rife with the opportunity for analysis - I see that now, whereas previously I couldn't get past how much I detested Holden as a character. ",

        "This is the book we all had to read back in high school, along with The Great Gatsby, because our English teachers felt a responsibility to impart to us an appreciation for great literature, even though all we cared about was making out and/or eating Gorditos in our friends' Plymouth Dusters while having deep discussions about the nature of humanity.Like The Great Gatsby, this is a book that flies right over the heads of teenagers, a book that can't really be fully understood by them because theThis is the book we all had to read back in high school, along with The Great Gatsby, because our English teachers felt a responsibility to impart to us an appreciation for great literature, even though all we cared about was making out and/or eating Gorditos in our friends' Plymouth Dusters while having deep discussions about the nature of humanity.Like The Great Gatsby, this is a book that flies right over the heads of teenagers, a book that can't really be fully understood by them because they lack the appropriate frame of reference. That's no fault of theirs -- the appropriate frame of reference for both novels (and so many others taught in so many high schools) is age. Most people don't \"get\" what's so great about Gatsby until they're in their 30s, and preferably, as in my case, have at least one divorce behind them to help put it all in perspective.Since I was unimpressed with Gatsby as a teen but came to adore it beyond all measure after my divorce proceedings were finally finalized, I figured I ought to give The Catcher in the Rye an adult shot, too.The result was a resounding \"MEH.\" It certainly wasn't bad, but it didn't grab me by the face and scream I AM RELEVANT TO YOUR EXPERIENCE in the same way Gatsby did. Perhaps that's because I found Holden Caulfield to be such an insufferable little shit. He starts out unpleasant and finishes unpleasant, and I never found myself really caring whether he made his breakthrough discoveries about life or himself or the nature of humanity while eating Gorditos in his friend's Plymouth Duster. He was just miserable to spend time with, and I realized with a sense of seasickness that I was on an accelerating time-warp, speeding toward my grave; that I had crossed some threshold somewhere back about five years ago, where, during a very special and very transient point in my existence, I could have really understood Holden and identified with him; where his wryness and darkness could have spoken to me. But now I was in my 30s, I had the debt of a divorce to pay off, a car payment, and an affair with a tattooed Coastie who lived on a boat in a seedy Seattle marina and who was two years younger than me -- and that latter fact thrilled me  than it would have thrilled a Holden Caulfield, and all I could really glean from his experience was an icky sense of entitlement and a vague sensation that I wanted him to get the hell off my lawn.I missed my Catcher in the Rye window, so I couldn't feel any  strongly about this book than three stars. Alas. I'm sure it's excellent if you read it at just the right point in your life.I recommend that you read it between the ages of 21 and 26. I also recommend that you have an affair with a tattooed Coastie who's two years younger than you and lives on a boat. Best to have said affair while reading The Great Gatsby. It's really a lot better than you remember from high school.P.s. The Coastie and I are still together and are very happy with our lives, and are living in a house, not on a boat in a seedy Seattle marina. Holden Caulfield would not approve, but I do.",

        "I don't often re-read books - a practice I'm hoping to change some as I expand my library - but Salinger's masterpiece was sitting on my shelf so I decided to give it a second look. Why? Well, it has been at least a decade since my first reading, and it was a boring, rainy day outside, and it came up in conversation once or twice during my time at Columbia, so I said hey, just knock this one out in an afternoon and see what you think. Well, one week later, here I am writing a review.Let's talk aI don't often re-read books - a practice I'm hoping to change some as I expand my library - but Salinger's masterpiece was sitting on my shelf so I decided to give it a second look. Why? Well, it has been at least a decade since my first reading, and it was a boring, rainy day outside, and it came up in conversation once or twice during my time at Columbia, so I said hey, just knock this one out in an afternoon and see what you think. Well, one week later, here I am writing a review.Let's talk about the voice first; that's where this book begins (and ends?) There's a reason young, brash first-person male narrators are always compared to Holden Caulfield - he's a memorable character. And of course, he's exceedingly unreliable throughout, he comes out and tells you how easy it is to lie to people, so that keeps the reader pleasantly off-balance. We also know from the first page that he's writing to us from some sort of treatment/rehab center, so clearly we have to read everything with a healthy dose of skepticism. I like that level of complexity. It never felt gimmicky to me. And the way that Salinger let the story unfold, with Holden sometimes giving us too much of some things (like calling up old Jane Alexander) and not enough of others (mostly in the resolution), I thought that was great. The story itself isn't all that interesting: Prep-school dropout goes on a bender in New York City, accompanied by cigarettes alcohol and his somewhat indifferent libido. Nothing terribly surprising happens, at least from my modern perspective. Maybe a 16-year old hiring a prostitute used to be a big deal, but that scene played out about as I expected (and maybe not just b/c I'd read it before?). Or maybe a venerated high school teacher coming onto his favorite student used to be a big deal, but that scene seemed pretty subdued now too. Nothing felt terribly outdated, don't get me wrong, but the story wasn't what kept me invested in the book.The book is a perfect length, and it covers the right amount of ground for this character. Any  time spent with Holden and his constant momentum swings on life, from \"everything is gorgeous\" to \"everything is lousy,\" would have been too much to stomach. But I think this book still holds up as a classic tale of the exhuberance and pain that comes with growing into adulthood. ",

        "The fact that this book didn't have a real plot was exactly why I enjoyed it. Holden is such a relatable, comforting, yet truly thought provoking character. The writing style of this book, as if the words came from Holden's head and onto the paper, was very refreshing and absolutely hilarious. However, I think the particular way in which he talks brings a light-hearted feel to the dark thoughts he has through out the book. I appreciate the fact that playful language was exactly what kept me fromThe fact that this book didn't have a real plot was exactly why I enjoyed it. Holden is such a relatable, comforting, yet truly thought provoking character. The writing style of this book, as if the words came from Holden's head and onto the paper, was very refreshing and absolutely hilarious. However, I think the particular way in which he talks brings a light-hearted feel to the dark thoughts he has through out the book. I appreciate the fact that playful language was exactly what kept me from crying myself blind and staring at a blank wall deep into the early hours of the morning, questioning all the change in my life and the big scary issues that I will have to face as an inventible part of growing up. Being a teen, I can relate to the feelings that Holden feels (minus all the shenanigans he gets up to): the loneliness, the waves of depression, the impulsive behaviour, and the bits of his past he randomly remembers which depress him even . There were three main themes, though, that I thought we very relatable; the isolation he feels, his desire/need to preserve his childhood and his fear of change. Even when Holden is talking to someone or hanging out with them, he still seems very alone and disconnected. The way he classifies people as \"phony\" and how he thinks of himself as the opposite is the wall that separates his desire for an \"intellectual\" conversation and the other person's desire, according to Holden, to show off. Throughout the whole book, Holden acts very childish and boyish, we also see his immaturity in the way he talks. The fact that he can't settle down at school and get serious about his studies, or how he can't seem to get intimate with a girl or deal with any physical contact at all, shows me, as a reader, how much Holden is holding on to his childhood, his innocents and his fear of responsibility and commitment, but he simply chalks it up to, \"not being in the mood\"Speaking of which, he is very indecisive, impulsive, spontaneous, and can't stick to the original plans he makes for himself. This is why it can be assumed that the plans he makes for his future: buying a cabin somewhere in the woods, pretending to be deaf-blind and never have another conversation again, marrying a deaf-blind girl, and not visiting home for a while, were never really going to happen and were all very unrealistic. Holden also has a connection to a girl he used to know when he was kid, Jane Gallagher. Throughout the book, Holden thinks about her and the time they spent together. He also thinks about calling her many times throughout his \"journey\" (if you'd even call it that) but always changes his mind. The book ends without us not really knowing what happens wth Jane. However, I think Jane symbolises Holden's childhood and a good, true, pure relationship. The reason why Holden never calls her up, is because he's scared she'll be a \"phony\" or she'll have changed into a person that Holden doesn't like, ruining the memories he has with her Holden's siblings also play a huge role in this book and dominate his thoughts, a lot of the time. D.B., his big brother, traded his raw talent for whatever was trending in Hollywood, just to please big producers and earn money. Holden believes that D.B. isn't true to himself and his talents and his original writing style, classifying him as, you guessed it, a \"phony\"His little brother, Allie, however, is the opposite. Allie died of leukemia when Holden was still a kid. Holden think so highly of Allie because all his memories with him are when they were kids. This is significant because Allie will never grow up to be a \"phony\", there is no risk, no possibility of Holden changing the way he sees Allie because he has passed away. His little sister, Phoebe is an important character because she is very smart and has a pure, kind nature. She is able to pick up on things that most kids don't notice and Holden is able to appreciate the same things as her and is able to hold a good conversation with her, which is something Holden struggles with throughout the book. Phoebe is also Holden's only sibling that doesn't abandon him. D.B. went off to Hollywood, Allie passed away, but Phoebe was the only person that could bring him joy. She kept him grounded and kept him from continuing to run away from his problems. Holden has such a fear of change and this is shown many times. For example, he continuously asks and thinks about the ducks in the Central Park lake and where they go for the winter. Holden seems very disturbed by the thought of having to change and alter your life in able to survive. Holden's fear of change is also shown with Holden's obsession with the museum. All the exhibits are encased in glass and, obviously, never change. He finds comfort in the fact that everything is exactly as it was the last time he visited it, many years ago. \"The best thing, though, in that museum was that everything always stayed right where it was. Nobody‚Äôd move. . . . Nobody‚Äôd be different. The only thing that would be different would be you.\"Holden's fear of change connects back to the theme of immaturity and his fear of growing up. He is afraid of the commitment and responsibility that comes with being a grown up. It could be argued that in order to be successful, you have to give in to what the general public will buy, you have to produce what will sell. This is seen in Holden's older brother, D.B. He goes off to Hollywood to pursue his writing career, but Holden feels D.B. is abandoning his original style or writing and is succumbing to the needs of his readers. This is the exact thing that Holden wants to avoid. He wants to achieve success by doing things he wants to do, things he likes doing and doing them on his own terms. This is the reason why Holden passes English and fails every other class. He clearly enjoys English, thus getting good grades and applying himself.  \"I‚Äôm standing on the edge of some crazy cliff. What I have to do, I have to catch everybody if they start to go over the cliff‚ÄîI mean if they‚Äôre running and they don‚Äôt look where they‚Äôre going I have to come out from somewhere and catch them. That‚Äôs all I‚Äôd do all day. I‚Äôd just be the catcher in the rye and all.\" This quote is from Holden's conversation with his sister Phoebe. This is his response when she asked him what he wants to do with his life, and it's also the quote that connects the title to the rest of the story. This quote makes he think that Holden sees himself as a protector of innocents. He is trying to keep all these kids safe from falling off the cliff. In other words, he is trying to preserve their innocents for as long as he can, maintaining his oversimplified view of the world. To him, children are easier to talk to because they are childish and naive, much like he is himself, whereas adults are hypocritical and superficial. He is actually able to get  out of a conversation with children, than he is with an adult. His catcher in the rye fantasy reflects his own innocence, his belief in pure, uncorrupted youth, and his desire to protect that spirit; on the other hand, it represents his extreme disconnection from reality and his ignorant, naive view of the world. All in all, as I'm sure you can tell by this long review, I REALLY enjoyed this book.",
        "It's hard to review this book, but I want to because I hate seeing people write mean things about it. When I first read it I was in the eighth-grade, and so naturally it was easier for me to connect with such a self-focused character. I'm glad that I read it when I was young, though; this is a book for the young. Not everybody is attracted to Holden once they are old enough to see how flawed his logic can sometimes be. He's hypocritical, judgmental, immature and over-sensitive. But he also has aIt's hard to review this book, but I want to because I hate seeing people write mean things about it. When I first read it I was in the eighth-grade, and so naturally it was easier for me to connect with such a self-focused character. I'm glad that I read it when I was young, though; this is a book for the young. Not everybody is attracted to Holden once they are old enough to see how flawed his logic can sometimes be. He's hypocritical, judgmental, immature and over-sensitive. But he also has a heart of gold, and the soul of a poet. He wants so badly to be sincere, and to feel that other people are sincere with him as well. That isn't how the world works, though, and he just can't reconcile himself with it yet. He's young. He's learning. That's part of what this book is about. Salinger has a magic touch that manages to fuse sadness and confusion with a dark humor that makes a long list of bitching and moaning funny enough to read. Holden has really felt these things- he is really admitting some of his most private pains- but he's a teenage boy, and so he has to confess them in an off-hand, flippant way. I think it's genius.I loved this book  than I have ever loved any book, and still love it so much that I almost feel personally insulted when people call it a pile of self-absorbed trash. It isn't. It has so much to offer; there are so many layers. If you find you can't connect with this book, you're either too old or too happy to be reading it now. Maybe you can't remember how it felt to be a confused, lonely kid with a bad mouth and good intentions. Or maybe you never were one. Either way, it has a lot  going on than plain old self indulgence. *Moreover, Holden's bourgeois background does not deny him the right to emotional discontent. I hate that argument. Perhaps instead it points to how little those comforts can absolve the pain of perceived intellectual misanthropy. He feels rejected, and he in turn rejects. Forever a classic. ",

I was supposed to be fighting for my life but I took moments aside to wish for his life to end!So, if you like this book, great! So this is one of those books that always makes me go, \"Really?\" but I understand that it's one that will have a different impact on the reader, depending on what place he or she is in life.",

### Best Quot Ever 

> Don't ever tell anybody anything, if you do, you start missing everybody
  J.D. Salinger
